Hunt weighed the angles. Russian skiffs were minutes out. Negotiation cost seconds he didn’t own. He faked a stumble to the left, letting his rifle dip, and Samir’s aim tracked him. Hunt lunged right, grabbing Samir’s wrist. The pistol discharged, bullet gouging the bulkhead. They wrestled, metal clanging. Hunt slammed Samir into the bulkhead, felt the younger man’s ribs crack, and twisted the gun free. He cuffed Samir’s hands with zip ties from his belt.

Barakat sagged in his chains. “Took you long enough,” he rasped.

Hunt sliced the restraints. “I was busy sabotaging your kidnappers,” he said. “Can you walk?”

“Define walk,” Barakat muttered, knees buckling. Hunt slung the officer’s arm over his shoulder, half-carrying him up the ladder while Samir stumbled behind, wrists bound.

They burst onto deck into a maelstrom. Russian RIBs knifed across the waves, PKM gunners scanning for targets. Hunt needed leverage. He grabbed a thermite charge, slapped it onto the refrigeration crate—where he suspected the guidance modules hid—and pulled the igniter. White-hot sparks cascaded. Metal groaned as the crate began to melt.

He shouted in Russian, voice amplified by a bullhorn he’d stolen from the Aswad. “Tell your captain to stay back. Another meter and I melt the rest of your payday.” The VolgaShield commander hesitated, clearly weighing profits versus pride.

Leyla rammed the *Artemis Dawn* alongside the Aswad, shouting in Arabic about “pirates with grenades” to maintain their smuggler cover. Hunt shoved Barakat across the gap, then hooked Samir’s restraints to a cargo net and dragged him after. Bullets stitched the air, punching holes in the smoke. Leyla gunned the throttle the instant Hunt’s boots hit her deck. The fishing boat surged away, leaving the Aswad drifting, one crate ablaze.

Hunt strapped Barakat into a folding seat, pressed a canteen to his lips. “Water,” he ordered.

Barakat drank, coughed, then managed a weak smile. “Always had a flair for the dramatic, Sam.”

“You’re welcome,” Hunt deadpanned. He turned to Samir, zip-tied ankles now bound to a cleat. “You just torched the last of your leverage. Mossad won’t give you a parade. They’ll give you paperwork and a cell.”

Samir’s glare held equal parts anger and despair. “History will vindicate me,” he muttered.

“History hates traitors,” Leyla called from the helm. “Ask any empire.”

Hunt scanned the horizon with binoculars. The Borey-12 hovered at the edge of Turkish airspace, unwilling to risk a firefight now that two Turkish F-16s streaked overhead, contrails carving the sky. Maverick’s logistics node burned behind them, smoke column marking their success like a flare.

Hunt pinged Maya. “Package secured: Barakat plus Samir. Guidance modules rendered molten. Exfiltrating toward Cyprus via civilian cover.”

“Copy,” Maya replied. “Cypriot Coast Guard owes me a favor. They’ll look the other way when you dock. Also, Moscow’s radio traffic just spiked. Expect propaganda soon.”

“They can call us pirates,” Hunt said. “We’ve been called worse.” He slid down beside Leyla, letting the Mediterranean spray salt his wounds. Ahead, the horizon softened. Behind, Cedar Crown’s maritime arm smoldered. For a moment, Act III belonged to them.

### 13. Thermite Sea
Flames chewed through the crates like termites through cedar. Each thermite puck Noura had smuggled out of Shahrazad burned white-hot, molten metal dripping onto the deck. Hunt crouched behind the Aswad’s wheelhouse, scanning past the heat shimmer. Two VolgaShield skiffs zigzagged closer, PKM gunners braced. Their captain likely assumed Hunt would never dare fire on Russians. Hunt liked disappointing assumptions.

He hefted the captured Igla launcher he’d yanked from a stunned Ashur guard. Instead of aiming at the trawler, he locked onto the seawater ahead of its bow. He fired. The missile screamed low, slammed into the waves twenty meters in front of the Borey-12, and detonated. A geyser of water and shrapnel rose, peppering the hull. The message was simple: *next one hits steel*. The skiffs wobbled. No VolgaShield contractor wanted to explain a dead American to Moscow.

He keyed his throat mic. “Yara, give me vectors,” he whispered. In Tripoli, the hacker piped satellite overlays into his HUD, painting wind arrows along the swells and tagging every AIS blip inside five nautical miles. Leyla flooded the Aswad’s VHF with Bedouin pop ballads so any Mayday would arrive garbled, then spun up her palm-sized ECM puck to fuzz the Borey-12’s fire-control radar. Hunt felt the hair on his arms rise as the jammers hissed—proof that their ad-hoc EW kit held for the moment.

Above, two Turkish F-16s roared past, afterburners painting the sky orange. Officially a training sortie; unofficially a warning. Maya had retasked them faster than bureaucracy should allow. Hunt waved at the trawler, mouthed, “Back off.” After a beat, the captain ordered reverse. Engines churned. The skiffs peeled away, still aiming guns but keeping distance.

Hunt tossed the empty launcher aside, grabbed Barakat under the arms, and dragged him toward the rail. The lieutenant’s coughs rattled. “You always dramatize extractions,” he wheezed.

“You always need rescuing,” Hunt shot back. He looped a line under Barakat’s torso, shoved him overboard, and dove after. Mediterranean salt burned every scrape. He swam hard toward the *Artemis Dawn*, where Leyla leaned over with a rope ladder.

They collapsed onto the deck as Leyla shoved the throttle forward. Behind them, the Aswad burned in two colors—orange diesel flames and the blinding white core of thermite. A column of black smoke marked the grave of Cedar Crown’s maritime phase.

Samir thrashed in the bilge, wrists zip-tied to a cleat. His eyes shone fever-bright. “You destroyed the insurance policy that could have kept Tel Aviv safe,” he spat.

Hunt tightened the ties around his ankles. “I kept Tel Aviv, Tehran, Riyadh, and a dozen other cities off someone’s ransom note,” he said. “You don’t get to decide which skyline burns because your conscience got impatient.”

Samir laughed bitterly. “Then who does? Langley? Saudi princes? Maverick? You?” His voice cracked on the last word.

“Ideally the people who build reactors for clinics, not extortion,” Hunt replied. He glanced at the photo of Noura taped inside his pack, the edges frayed. “Until then, I break things so she can keep building.”

Leyla pointed starboard. “Borey-12 is loitering on the horizon,” she reported. “Give it ten minutes and they’ll call in a frigate with a diplomatic flag. We need to vanish.”

Hunt nodded. He checked Barakat, who was already plotting revenge in hushed Arabic. “Feel like interviewing our Mossad friend?” Hunt asked.

Barakat’s grin was wicked. “I owe him some questions about loyalty.” He hauled Samir below deck toward the cramped cabin. Hunt didn’t ask what interrogation method he’d use. Plausible deniability tasted like clean water after a long patrol.

Before following, Hunt ducked into the engine bay, slapped two more sugar-gel cartridges onto the intake manifold, and wired them to a dead-man switch zip-tied under the helm. “If VolgaShield boards, we scuttle,” he told Leyla. She winced but nodded. He also slid an SD card of helmet-cam footage into a hollow winch bolt—evidence cache in case the portal servers went dark. Layers on layers; Clancy would approve.

Hunt slid into the co-pilot seat beside Leyla, scanning the radar for additional blips. “Next stop Cyprus,” he said.

Leyla arched a brow. “Maverick will expect you there.”

“He expects us everywhere,” Hunt replied. “Better to be unpredictable by being omnipresent.” He keyed the sat radio. “Shipment destroyed. Barakat safe. Samir in custody. Vectoring to Limassol. Need Trident Holdings intel and customs liaison.”

Maya answered instantly. “Inspector Elena Vassos in Limassol owes me a favor. She controls the bonded warehouses. She’ll meet you at Pier 12. Also, Prince Talal bin Masri is panicking—moving crypto through offshore wallets. If you snag ledger proof, Riyadh might finally act. And yes, those were my F-16s. You owe me a bottle of Zahle arak.”

“Add it to my debt,” Hunt said. He watched the wreckage recede, smoke spiraling into a sky already cluttered with contrails. One branch cut from Cedar Crown’s tree. The forest still stood. “On to the next trunk,” he murmured, cracking his knuckles—a Reacher habit he’d accidentally adopted.

### 14. Ghost Debrief
They disappeared into Gemmayzeh, up a staircase hidden behind a shuttered French bookstore whose proprietor owed Leyla three favors. Dust motes floated in the stale air. The smell of salt, ink, and ozone clung to the walls. Hunt, Leyla, Noura, and Barakat huddled around a flickering holo projector projecting Maya Trask’s face from a vault somewhere beneath Langley. Samir sat bound in a corner, gag removed so he could answer questions if needed.

“You melted their guidance package,” Maya began without pleasantries. “Good. But Maverick countered with a manifesto. Video’s already on twelve networks. He labeled you a rogue asset, spliced in footage from Mosul, Caracas, Caracas again—he even doctored feeds to show you executing prisoners. Every intelligence service now wants to ‘debrief’ you. Half want you dead.”

Hunt shrugged. “Tell them to take a number.”

Maya scowled. “Focus. Cedar Crown’s financing line runs through Prince Talal bin Masri. He’s laundering money via a Cypriot shelf called Trident Holdings. We need incontrovertible proof to shove under Riyadh’s nose. Inspector Elena Vassos at Limassol customs is the key. She’ll cooperate if we give her leverage.”

Leyla’s datapad chimed with an encrypted deposit. Hunt raised an eyebrow. “Selling my exfil routes?”

“Selling old ones,” she shot back. “Money buys buses for refugees stuck in Deir ez-Zor. They need fuel more than you need trust issues. Be grateful I didn’t auction your blood type.”

Barakat snorted over his tea. “She smuggled my wounded across three borders while you sulked in Virginia, Sam. Maybe trust her expertise.”

Hunt bit back a retort. “Fair,” he conceded. “But if one coin lands in Talal’s wallet, I’ll hang it around your neck.”

Noura, wrapped in a gray blanket, cleared her throat. “What about Samir?” she asked softly. Exhaustion hollowed her eyes. “He saved me twice. He also betrayed everyone twice.”

“Mossad already disavowed him,” Maya said. “They’ll let us keep him for now. But don’t underestimate their appetite for retrieval. Keep him restrained. Meanwhile, Noura, I need you to model the cascading failure if Cedar Crown tries to rebuild the guidance tree with missing components.”

Noura nodded. “I’ll need lab equipment. Stable power. Preferably silence.”

“We’ll set up a lab in Limassol,” Hunt promised. “Leyla, reach out to Elena. Barakat, you escort Noura and babysit Samir. I’ll coordinate flights and scrub the manifests Maverick leaked.”

Leyla flicked open a holo-map of the Cypriot freeport, its bonded warehouses color-coded. “Pier 12 has blind CCTV for thirty seconds whenever the cruise ferries dock,” she said. “Elena can park us there, but we need cover paperwork—antique weapons restoration exhibit, maybe?” Hunt nodded, already drafting a manifest that mixed genuine artifacts from his Jerusalem stash with hollow crates for kit. Barakat listed sling-load weights for Noura’s lab gear, muttering about customs inspectors who measured everything twice.

Samir watched quietly, eyes calculating. Hunt noticed and leaned in. “Memorize every map,” he warned. “If you run, I’ll post them to Mossad’s holiday mailing list.” Samir looked away first.

Samir scoffed. “You can’t keep me forever.”

Hunt crouched so their eyes were level. “No. One day you’ll walk free. But when you do, Mossad, Riyadh, Tehran, Langley, and Maverick will all know you betrayed them. That’s not freedom. That’s a death sentence served slow.”

Samir swallowed.

Maya cut the feed. Static filled the room. Outside, Beirut traffic blared, ordinary and oblivious. Hunt rubbed his eyes. “We move at 0400,” he said. “Cyprus first, Tripoli after. Maverick will drop another video before sunset; let him. We’ll give the world evidence.”

Leyla stretched, spine popping. “Fifty-two hours awake,” she muttered. “Guinness should issue a certificate.”

“Sleep on the charter plane,” Hunt said. “Bring your shotgun; Cypriot customs love confiscating souvenirs.” He walked to the shuttered balcony, watched two teenagers argue about soccer. He smiled despite himself. The world spun, unaware of the thermite still cooling in the Mediterranean. He intended to keep it spinning.

### 15. Fault Lines
Beirut woke to Maverick’s hologram dominating every LED billboard from Martyrs’ Square to Pigeon Rocks. He wore a bespoke suit, hair perfect, voice honeyed. “Samuel Hunt,” he declared, “serves no country. He ignites wars to feed his ego.” The broadcast cut between real footage—Hunt hauling civilians through Mosul rubble—and manufactured clips of him firing into crowds. Hashtags trended globally. Commentators speculated whether he was hero or terrorist. Maverick’s psyops landed like artillery.

Hunt met Leyla atop the cliffs overlooking Pigeon Rocks. Waves crashed below, sending spray high into the morning haze. Leyla wore mirrored sunglasses even though clouds muted the sun. “Your fan club grows,” she said dryly.

“Maverick wants me reactive,” Hunt replied. “He herds enemies by giving them a mirror. If I chase every narrative, I stop hunting him.”

Leyla pulled a cigarette from a tin, then thought better and tucked it away. “The deposit I got last night came from a Saudi banker tied to Talal. He wanted convoy intel. I sold him stale routes, but if he’s Talal’s fixer, he’ll plant the tracker you gave me.”

Hunt produced a new GPS bead disguised as a brass earring. “Clip this to your scarf when you meet him. It pings every thirty seconds on burst. Maybe we track Talal’s courier to his bolt-hole.”

She clipped it without hesitation. “You finally trust me with your shiny toys.”

“I trust you to be ruthlessly practical,” Hunt said. “Which is what this mission needs.”

They watched fishermen cast lines into gray water, oblivious to the shadow war. After a beat, Leyla asked quietly, “Why keep doing this, Sam? You could vanish to Crete, open a beach bar, tell stories to tourists. Let the agencies clean their own mess.”

Hunt stared at the horizon where oil tankers queued. “Because men like Maverick thrive when good people check out. They colonize the vacuum. Someone has to close the gaps.” He thought of Reacher—how the drifter would simply break knees and move on. Hunt didn’t have that luxury. He had Maya, Noura, Leyla, Barakat—an accidental coalition depending on him.

His satphone buzzed. Maya again. “Tripoli hacker collective ‘Server Dust’ flagged a blockchain ledger flowing into Trident Holdings,” she said. “It’s hidden behind a barbershop that doubles as a crypto mine. Elena can raid it if we hand her proof. Also, Prince Talal’s cousin, Saeed, lands in Beirut tonight. He’s their bagman. Tag him if you can.”

“Roger,” Hunt said. He looked at Leyla. “Pack the mobile lab. We hit Tripoli’s souqs by noon, dig out the ledger, then intercept Saeed at the Phoenicia.”

Leyla groaned. “So no nap.”

“Sleep when Cedar Crown dies,” Hunt replied. They walked toward the Hilux. On the way, two teenagers shyly approached. “Are you the guy from the hologram?” one asked.

Hunt considered lying. Instead he said, “Do your homework. Don’t believe every video.” They blinked, baffled. Leyla laughed. “You’re terrible at PR.”

“I’m worse at funerals,” he said, climbing into the truck. He glanced at the skyline—the same one Maverick threatened to rewrite. “Tripoli first. Then Talal.” He gripped the wheel until the leather creaked. Challenges kept him honest. He liked honest.

By nightfall they prowled the Phoenicia lobby under forged NGO badges, waiting for Saeed’s jet to disgorge him. Leyla’s tracker earring gleamed faintly under her scarf. When Saeed strode in—tailored thawb, entourage of two—she “tripped,” spilling espresso onto his attaché case. Hunt rushed in with apologies, slipping the brass bead into the case’s hinge before security even realized there’d been contact. Saeed waved them off, more annoyed about his robe than strangers. Five minutes later, Yara texted: **Tracker live. He’s headed to a rooftop suite with its own satellite uplink.** Hunt exhaled. Another thread to yank when the Tripoli ledger fell into their lap.

#### Interlude 05 – Maverick’s Broadcast Control
Somewhere inside a luxury penthouse overlooking the Bosphorus, Maverick stood before a wall of monitors. A half-dozen technicians typed furiously, deepfaking footage frame by frame. Maverick adjusted his cufflinks, gaze fixed on a live feed of Hunt at Pigeon Rocks. “Zoom,” he ordered. The image tightened. “Overlay the wildfire footage. Blend the colors. I want sympathy and suspicion in equal measure.”

His comms chief—codename Leyland—hesitated. “Public opinion might swing toward pity,” he warned. “Too much martyrdom, we lose control.”

Maverick smiled. “People love a fallen hero. They’ll watch closer. Attention is oxygen.” He tapped an interactive map. New nodes glowed—Tripoli, Limassol, Mykonos. “Samuel thinks chopping branches slows the tree. He forgets I planted seeds everywhere. Phase Sahara, Phase Aegean, Phase Atlas—they bloom while he douses one fire.”

He keyed a secure line. Prince Talal’s face appeared, pale. “Your asset destroyed the sea shipment,” Maverick said calmly. “I need new capital. Shift funds from Trident Holdings to the ‘Atlas’ accounts. And send Saeed to Beirut with the ledger backup. Watch the tracker Leyla plants; she can’t resist shiny devices.”

Talal swallowed. “And Hunt?”

“Let him chase ghosts,” Maverick said. “The more the world watches him, the easier it is to hide in plain sight.” He ended the call, studied Hunt’s image one more time, and whispered, “Run faster, Sam. I built mazes for men like you.” He signaled Leyland. The next broadcast queued up, ready to flood the Levant with lies.

## Act IV – Cedar Crown Gambit

### 16. Trident Array
Tripoli’s Souk el-Masriya smelled of cardamom, diesel, and rain. Behind stalls hawking saffron and prayer beads lurked an entirely different market: encrypted wallets and zero-day exploits. The portal sat behind **Fade Nation**, a barbershop with chrome chairs and NBA posters. Hunt and Leyla entered like customers. The barber—a teenager with luminescent blue hair—caught Leyla’s eye, flipped a hidden switch, and the mirror slid aside to reveal a stairwell pulsing with neon.

They descended into a basement humming with fans. Server racks glowed purple. Beanbags littered the floor beside 3D printers extruding plastic drone housings. Leyla fist-bumped Yara Khoury, the underground’s reigning sysadmin. Yara wore circuit-diagram tattoos down both arms and chewed 3ala gum as if it were intel. “You’re late,” she said. “Talal’s wallets pinged us twice already. He routes cash through Trident Holdings in Limassol, into NFT galleries named after saints. Cute, right?”

Hunt crouched beside her rig. Blockchains cascaded across six monitors. He produced a battered USB stick containing relic malware from the Stuxnet days. “Need manifests flagged as medical but cold-chain. Preferably with diplomatic immunity stamps.”

Yara arched a brow. “Vintage spyware? Respect.” She plugged it in, isolated it inside a sandbox, and watched it chew through Trident’s firewalls. Honeypots blinked. She smirked. “They’re sloppy. All vanity NFTs and no salting.”

Lines of code scrolled. A ledger opened—shipments labeled **CT SCANNERS** and **RADIATION THERAPY UNITS**, each tied to container IDs bound for Limassol’s bonded zone. Hunt cross-referenced the IDs with the GPS breadcrumbs Samir secretly left behind in Wadi Sirhan. Coordinates matched. Cedar Crown had swapped desert trucks for freeport cranes.

Maya whispered through Hunt’s earpiece. “We need Talal’s personal signature. Riyadh only acts when embarrassed.”

Hunt dug deeper, using the Stuxnet worm to pivot into a hidden sub-ledger tagged **ARAR-TRIDENT FUND**. There it was: Talal bin Masri’s private account pushing crypto through a Turkish fintech shell, then laundering it via a UAE metaverse casino. Each transfer bore his biometric signature. Hunt scraped everything, double-encrypted the cache, and fired it to Maya. “Gift wrapped.”

“State will frame it as counter-proliferation,” Maya replied. “But we still have to seize the boxes. Limassol inspectors need probable cause they can brag about.”

Hunt wiped the intrusion trail, bricking the sacrificial server. Alarms wailed, bathing the basement in red light. Yara groaned. “Seriously? You tripped their fail-safe.”

“You’re welcome,” Hunt said. “Now they can’t trace you.” He slid a stack of euros across the table. “Buy new fans. It’s hot down here.”

They bolted up the stairs as the barber casually trimmed a customer’s hair. Outside, Leyla checked her phone. The GPS bead she’d clipped to Saeed bin Masri—the prince’s cousin—pulsed alive. “Phoenicia Hotel rooftop, 2100 hours,” she said. “He’s making a handoff.”

“He can wait,” Hunt said. “Containers can’t.” He texted Inspector Elena Vassos in Cyprus: **Need warrant, Warehouse 17. Arrive tomorrow. Bring bolt cutters and sarcasm.** She replied with a thumbs-up emoji followed by a middle finger. Hunt chuckled. He admired officials who accessorized their compliance.

### 17. Ghost Rail
Flying out of Tripoli would light up every watchlist on two continents, so Leyla scored four berths on a smuggler freight that rumbled from the Beka’a Valley down to the Syrian littoral. The train hauled citrus crates, diesel tanks, and stolen antiquities destined for crooked collectors. Hunt rode in an empty flatcar beside pallets of olive oil. Leyla hid two cars back with the makeshift comms kit. Noura and Barakat bunkered near the center with radiation sensors, while Samir—zip-tied and gagged—wriggled miserably inside a padded instrument crate.

Thunderheads stacked over the valley. Rain hammered the cars, turning the roof slick. Hunt sat cross-legged, counting the rhythm of the rails to stay alert. Halfway through the gorge, the compartment door slid open. Maverick stepped in as if boarding a commuter line, suppressed AK slung casual, rain beading off his waxed jacket. Beside him stood Saeed bin Masri in a tailored suit, and—impossibly—Samir, wrists free, smirking through bruises. Leyla knelt with wrists zip-tied, a curved blade at her throat courtesy of one of Maverick’s mercs.

“Miss me?” Maverick purred. His accent had softened over the years, now a blend of Riyadh finishing school and Delta barracks. “You stole my reactor. I steal your fixer.”

Hunt raised his palms. “Let her walk and we talk.”

“No,” Maverick said. “Roof. Now. Bring the ledger you grabbed from Tripoli. Or I repaint the tracks with her blood.”

Hunt climbed the ladder onto the roof, rain slashing his face. The train roared across a viaduct, wind threatening to peel them off. Maverick shoved Leyla ahead like a shield. Lightning lit the gorge, white-hot. “Phase II is already inside Cypriot bonded zones,” Maverick shouted over the wind. “Phase III leaves Casablanca next week. You can’t outrun math.”

“I don’t outrun it,” Hunt replied. “I break the pencil.” He lunged, ramming his shoulder into Maverick’s chest. They slid across the wet roof. Leyla rolled free, booting Saeed in the knee. The prince’s cousin tumbled, clutching the GPS bead as it blinked. Good—Leyla’s tracker stayed attached.

Samir scrambled up from the hatch, knife in hand. “You can’t save everyone!” he screamed, voice raw. “Pick a city, Hunt!”

Hunt kicked the knife away, twisted Samir’s wrist until tendons popped, then shoved him toward the flatcar’s edge. Samir clung to a vent, boots dangling over black nothing. His eyes were wide, terror finally eclipsing ideology. “Please—”

Lightning struck the rails ahead. The train lurched. Samir lost his grip and vanished into rain and stone. Hunt’s stomach clenched. Maybe the kid landed in mud and lived. Maybe he didn’t. War rarely gave yes-or-no answers.

Maverick headbutted Hunt, blood spraying. Hunt countered with a palm strike that shattered the mercenary’s nose. The rifle skidded away. Maverick rolled, vaulted to the next car, and jumped toward a waiting helicopter rope ladder dangling out of the storm. Hunt sprinted, fingers brushing his boot, but the downdraft shoved him back. Leyla grabbed his harness, yanking him from the edge.

“He’s airborne,” she shouted.

Hunt spat blood, chest heaving. “Then we follow the money to the landing zone.” He stared into the rain. Maverick now knew their timeline. Fine. Ghost rails cut both ways.

### 18. Freeport Siege
Limassol’s freeport spread across reclaimed shoreline like a sovereign city-state: razor wire, biometric gates, bonded warehouses with names like **Helios Logistics** and **MedCare Holding**. Hunt met Inspector Elena Vassos at dawn amid a maze of shipping containers stacked six high. She wore a floral dress under a ballistic vest, cigarette balanced on her lower lip. “You better have art,” she said in English seasoned with Greek consonants. “My boss loves paperwork.”

Hunt handed over the Trident manifests, Talal’s crypto ledger, and a printed copy of Noura’s radiation modeling. “Medical cover loads. Diplomatic seals. Probable cause squared.”

Elena scanned the documents, smirked, and flicked her cigarette toward the sea. “Call me when this becomes a scandal so I get promoted.” She radioed her team. Cypriot police formed wedges at Warehouse 17’s three entrances. NATO auditors hovered nearby, hungry for headlines.

Hunt launched a quadcopter, feeding video to Leyla (parked outside with a jammer), Barakat (coordinating Jordanian liaison), and Noura (monitoring radiation detectors). He felt the familiar calm of pre-breach rituals. “On my mark,” he said.

Flashbangs thumped. Hunt’s team flowed inside, boots thudding on polished concrete. Crates labeled **MEDICAL EQUIPMENT – FRAGILE** towered overhead. Thermal scans showed nothing hotter than coffee. Suspicious. He pried a crate open. Inside sat lead bricks wrapped around cheap routers to spoof radiation signatures. A shell game.

Leyla crackled over comms. “Found two engineers zip-tied near the loading dock. They say they were brought in to calibrate shielding, got locked up when they started asking why the ‘clinic’ needed anti-sabotage foam.”

“Exfil them,” Hunt ordered. He stalked deeper, spotting fiber conduits snaking toward a secure server cage. His earpiece hissed with Maverick’s voice. “Thank you for revealing where NATO hides their cameras,” Maverick purred. “Now I know where to blind them.”

Hunt swore. Maverick wanted the raid to succeed just enough to expose sensors. Hunt moved fast, jacked a fiber optic tap into the network, and mirrored the encrypted traffic blasting toward an offshore server. “If I can’t seize your core, I’ll burn your cover,” he muttered, shunting the packets to Maya. She pinged back: **Recording. We’ll cross-reference with Riyadh. Keep collecting.**

Elena barged over, smoke trailing. “Where’s the reactor?” she demanded.

“Already shipped east,” Hunt said. “But we just nailed the financing trail and the IT backbone. Talal can’t claim ignorance.”

Elena spat onto the concrete. “I hate princes.”

Noura hugged the freed engineers, promising the Cedar Coalition would find them real work. Barakat relayed updates to Jordanian intelligence. Outside, NATO auditors filmed everything, eager for a leak. Hunt watched container cranes swing gracefully over the harbor. Somewhere beyond the breakwater a vessel carried the real Cedar Crown package. He had hours, maybe less, to change course.

He keyed Maya. “Need a fresh lead. This warehouse was theater.”

“Already on it,” Maya replied. “We cracked Maverick’s satphone. He’s meeting Prince Talal tonight at the Phoenicia Hotel’s rooftop helipad. Riyadh wants deniability. We’ll give them proof on live stream.”

Hunt cracked his knuckles, a habit he’d mocked in Reacher novels and now imitated without shame. “Then Beirut gets a show,” he said. “Tell Elena to prep arrest forms. We’re bringing a prince to class.”

#### Interlude 06 – Elena’s Memo
**From:** Inspector Elena Vassos  
**To:** Cyprus Anti-Proliferation Task Force  
**Subject:** Warehouse 17 raid – Addendum

Per joint operation with allied contractor “Samuel Hunt,” we executed warrants on Trident Holdings’ bonded storage. Physical seizure yielded decoy freight only; however, we obtained:

1. Ledger extracts tying HRH Talal bin Masri (Saudi Arabia) to the ARAR-TRIDENT fund. Signed copies attached.  
2. Recorded network traffic between Warehouse 17 and offshore node 34.112.98.17 (flagged for Russian PMC ties).  
3. Two engineers (Ibrahim F., Sofia K.) willing to testify that “medical” orders were, in fact, modular reactor housings.

Recommendation: forward evidence to EUCOM and Riyadh simultaneously. Force Talal to choose between public humiliation and cooperation. Also, please reimburse me for the floral vest ruined by thermite residue. Smoking on duty remains necessary.

—Elena

### 19. Public Burn
The Phoenicia Hotel rose over the Corniche like a throne. Prince Talal bin Masri had rented the entire top floor to mourn his collapsing offshore empire. Hunt occupied the adjacent rooftop with Leyla, Barakat, and a rolling pelican case full of fiber taps. Through a spotting scope he watched Talal pace in silk robes while Maverick lounged on the sofa, shoes on the table, eyes glued to half a dozen muted news feeds.

Leyla lay prone beside Hunt, Barrett balanced on a sandbag with an EXACTO guidance canister clipped to the rail so the .50-cal dart rounds could read her laser spot and ride its beam. The optical seeker in each round blinked green inside her scope overlay, meaning the on-board fins could make dozens of course corrections each second even if the Corniche winds shifted.citeturn2search0turn2search3 “One suppressed round and the Cedar Coalition gets the night off,” she whispered.

“Tempting,” Hunt said. “But Riyadh needs a disgraced prince alive if we want Phase Two funding to dry up.” Below them, Barakat crouched over a jammer rig patched into a hotel maintenance junction box. Noura and Yara worked from a borrowed command van, stitching together overlays in real time. Elena Vassos had even flown in from Cyprus, disguised as Talal’s “wellness consultant,” with a warrant taped under her blouse.

Maya patched into Hunt’s earpiece. “Fiber tap is live. Billboards ready. When you flip the switch, every LED in Beirut becomes a deposition.”

Hunt unspooled a thin cable, clipped it into the hotel’s rooftop satellite dish, and connected a tablet. He pulled up the Limassol recordings, layered in Talal’s crypto ledger, added maps of Trident shipments and stills of the kidnapped engineers. He timed the release to coincide with the 2100 call to prayer; when the city fell momentarily quiet, he hit SEND.

Every digital billboard from Gemmayzeh to Pigeon Rocks lit with Maverick’s studio-quality confession. Subtitles rolled in Arabic, English, and Turkish. Talal’s personal signature pulsated on-screen next to wire transfers. Hasan, the blue-haired barber, piped the feed onto social media with hashtags Leyla had pre-seeded. Phones across the city vibrated in unison. In Riyadh, the Royal Court issued a communiqué cutting Talal off within minutes. Ankara demanded Moscow explain VolgaShield’s contracts. Brussels hinted at sanctions. Beirut’s streets thrummed with shouts as people pointed upward at the shamed prince’s face.

Inside the suite, Maverick leapt to his feet, complexion draining. “What did you do?” Talal screamed, hurling a crystal decanter. Maverick reached for his satphone. Barakat flicked on the jammer. The call died. Hunt keyed his radio. “Maverick, window six.”

Leyla fired once. The suppressed round shattered the reinforced glass inches from Maverick’s ear. He dove for cover, dragging Talal down. Hunt’s voice boomed from the hijacked hotel PA: “Prince Talal bin Masri, you are under arrest for financing nuclear terrorism. The whole world is watching.”

Lebanese Internal Security—alerted by Elena’s warrant—flooded the hallway. Elena herself led the team, floral dress now covered by a tac vest. They breached the suite, zip-tied Talal, and hauled him out past gawking staff. Cypriot police, acting on Elena’s earlier tip, simultaneously raided Talal’s Limassol villas, seizing art, accounts, and—crucially—the notary who handled Cedar Crown shell companies.

Maverick slipped through a hidden catering corridor that Hunt had flagged hours earlier. He sprinted toward the helipad, but Leyla had already peeled from the rooftop. She planted a magnetic GPS puck under the helicopter skid while disguised as a flight attendant, then vanished down the stairwell. When Hunt reached the helipad, the rotors were already spinning. Maverick clung to the side ladder, blood dripping from the cut Leyla’s bullet had carved along his jaw. “See you in Casablanca, Sam!” he shouted over the rotor wash. Hunt fired a burst; rounds sparked across the fuselage. The helicopter banked north, GPS beacon blinking on Hunt’s tablet.

Back at their Jounieh safe house, the team watched the cascade. Riyadh froze Talal’s assets and issued an arrest warrant. VolgaShield announced a “temporary pause” in Mediterranean contracts. EUCOM invited the Cedar Coalition to brief them. Maya called, exhaustion edging her voice. “You set three capitals on fire in one night,” she said. “Congratulations, celebrity. There’s now a bounty on you in five languages.”

Hunt lined up magazines on the table, methodical. “Add it to the scrapbook,” he replied.

Leyla tossed a go-bag onto the couch. “Talal’s money is gone. Maverick still has reactors. What’s next?”

Hunt stared at the corkboard covered in pins and yarn. “We follow the helicopter trail. Casablanca, Oman, maybe Karachi. Until he runs out of air.”

Noura, typing equations into a rugged laptop, looked up. “And when we catch him, I testify. Live. No more shadows.”

“You’ll get your stage,” Hunt promised. But even as he said it, the tracker on his tablet blinked farther west. Phase Two was moving. He flexed his hands, knuckles popping. “No rest,” he murmured. “Not yet.”

#### Interlude 07 – Royal Court Bulletin
**Classification:** TOP SECRET // KSA INTERNAL  
**From:** Minister of State Fahd al-Hamidi  
**To:** Custodian of the Two Holy Mosques, Defense Council  
**Subject:** Immediate Action – Prince Talal bin Masri

1. Verified video evidence (broadcast 2100 Beirut time) links HRH Talal bin Masri to clandestine payments labeled ARAR-TRIDENT FUND. Transfers routed through Riyadh Commercial Bank, 14 February – 26 March.  
2. Recorded confession features foreign national “Maverick” (alias). Forensic voice print confirms same speaker tied to Cedar Crown manifesto intercepted 17 February.  
3. Recommendation:  
   a. Issue royal decree removing Talal from all councils.  
   b. Freeze assets, seize passports of immediate family.  
   c. Quietly authorize cooperation with Cedar Coalition task force led by Samuel Hunt (unofficial asset).  
4. Public statement drafted for release within the hour emphasizing Kingdom’s zero tolerance for nuclear adventurism.  
5. Note: Intelligence indicates Maverick now coordinates with Levon Markarian in North Africa. Prepare liaison with Moroccan DGST.

Respectfully,  
F. al-Hamidi

### 20. Sea Glass Endgame
Forty-eight hours later, the Corniche glowed gold at sunset. Hunt, Leyla, Noura, Barakat, and Elena gathered on a rooftop strewn with comms gear and notebooks. Below them, Beirut returned to its default rhythm—motorbikes weaving, fishermen hauling nets, the call to prayer echoing between towers. Above them, a swarm of microdrones hovered, mapping maritime traffic toward Malta and Tangier.

Leyla poured arak into chipped tea glasses. “To Talal’s frozen assets,” she said.

Elena clinked her glass. “And to paperwork,” she added, deadpan. Everyone laughed, tension cracking for the first time in days.

Noura unfolded blueprints across a crate. “Phase Two isn’t just Casablanca,” she said. “My intercepts show components staged in Muscat and Gwadar. If we launch the Cedar Coalition publicly, we can crowdsource inspectors. Think of it as open-source safeguards.” She pointed at a schematic labeled **Cedar Coalition Portal**—a mesh of refugee clinics, universities, and cargo inspectors sharing data via encrypted channels Leyla and Yara were building.

Hunt listened, letting their energy seep in. His phone buzzed. Maya’s text: **Extraction bird wheels up at 2300. Seat for you if you finally want out.** He stared at the words. Leyla watched his expression. “She won’t stop asking,” she said softly. “Someday you might be tired enough to say yes.”

Hunt typed back: **Pass. Need Phase Two intel instead.**  
Maya replied within a minute. **Casablanca Freeport. Container ID IFU-7713. Cover story: industrial filtration. Tracker shows route toward Nouakchott. Levon Markarian rumored on site.**

Leyla groaned. “I just washed the salt off my boots.”

“You’ll get more sand soon,” Hunt said. He unfolded a satellite print of Morocco’s coast. “We hit Casablanca, then shadow the convoy across the Atlas. Barakat, prep Jordanian liaison with DGST. Noura, push your open-source initiative live before dawn. Elena, brief Interpol so when we seize Phase Two, they already have warrants.”

Noura squeezed his shoulder. “One day you’ll run out of excuses to keep fighting,” she murmured.

Hunt looked out toward the Mediterranean, where oil tankers bobbed like patient beasts. “Maybe,” he said. “Until then, I stay noisy so Maverick never sleeps.”

They sat in silence, watching the sea glass shimmer. Below them, street vendors hawked roasted corn, oblivious to the war humming overhead. Hunt inhaled diesel, spice, salt—a cocktail that felt like home. “Gather the Coalition,” he said finally. “We fly at dawn.”

Leyla raised her glass once more. “To monsters who hunt bigger monsters,” she toasted. They drank. The arak burned, sharp and clean. Hunt stood, slung his rifle, and headed for the stairs. Leyla, Noura, Elena, and Barakat followed. Tomorrow they would chase a container through Morocco. Tonight they allowed themselves fifteen minutes to breathe.

## Act V – Phase Sahara

### 21. Atlas Vectors
Casablanca’s freeport inhaled the Atlantic and exhaled freight. Container canyons rose six stories high, painted in shipping line colors that glowed under sodium lights. Hunt felt the hum of gantries in his bones as he walked beside DGST officer Amina Haddad. She wore a charcoal suit, sneakers, and the amused expression of someone who knew exactly how much chaos Hunt would cause.

“Container IFU-7713 offloaded two hours ago,” Amina said, handing him a rugged tablet. “Manifest says industrial filtration units destined for Nouakchott desalination. Actual thermal signature says ‘don’t touch without lead gloves.’ We staged it in Bay Delta under camera ‘maintenance.’ You have twenty minutes before customs starts asking rude questions.”

“FUGAZI manifest,” Hunt muttered. “Fouled up beyond recognition, just shiny enough to trick auditors.”

Leyla, already in dockworker coveralls, climbed a reach stacker and plugged a wafer into its telemetry bus. “I’ll control the lifts,” she radioed. “Yara’s mesh network is live; Coalition volunteers are flagging any deviations on social feeds.” Down on the quay, Noura and Elena set up portable radiation detectors disguised as luggage scales. Barakat liaised with DGST commandos hidden inside an empty reefer.

Hunt circled the container, fingers brushing rivets. Leyla’s drainage-plug tracker blinked under his palm—proof the package matched the yacht intel. He slid a boroscope through a bolt hole. Inside, carbon foam cradled a modular core, coolant lines coiled like serpents. He fought down a surge of anger. Cedar Crown had cloned the Shahrazad design perfectly.

“We can’t grab it here,” Noura whispered. “Moroccan law requires diplomatic note. Talal’s lawyers will stall.”

“Then we ghost it,” Hunt said. “Swap the RFID seals, inject our own telemetry, and ride shotgun to the Atlas.” He pulled out a pouch of Leyla’s sugar gel. “I’ll seed the escort trucks. Make them sputter halfway up the pass so we can isolate the core. If this goes FUBAR, at least we know whose fingerprints are on the crate.”

Amina raised an eyebrow. “Sugar sabotage? Very Casablanca noir.”

Hunt grinned. “You should see the sequel.”

They split up. Leyla hijacked the port’s automatic stacking cranes, sliding the container into a convoy lane flanked by humanitarian trucks. Noura faked a radiation alarm on a different crate, buying them another ten minutes. Hunt crawled under the lead MAN truck, inserting gel cartridges into coolant ports and wiring a remote trigger. Barakat distributed Moroccan police uniforms to Coalition operatives who would man the next checkpoint.

As the convoy rolled out, Hunt planted microdrones on lampposts, feeding video back to Amina’s ops room. “Atlas pass winds like a cobra,” she warned. “Wind gusts up to forty knots tonight. Perfect for an ambush if you like falling off cliffs.”

“I prefer rooftops,” Hunt said. “But I’ll take cliffs.”

They pulled back to the command van parked near Hassan II Mosque. Inside, Yara piped DGST feeds onto a wall of monitors. Forty Coalition volunteers across North Africa reported via encrypted chat. The Cedar Coalition portal Noura launched earlier flickered with whistleblower tips—some junk, some gold.

“Convoy cleared Gate 4,” Leyla reported. “Sensors show standard radiation levels. No tampering detected.”

“Because we haven’t tampered yet,” Hunt said. He looked at the map peppered with trackers: IFU-7713 blinking, decoy trucks pulsing. “Phase Sahara thinks we’ll chase the obvious. Let’s make obvious hurt.”

He sent a burst to Maya: **Atlas intercept go. Need overhead from Pleiades pass 04:17Z.** Within seconds, satellites retasked. The convoy’s heat signatures sharpened on the screen. Hunt flexed his hands. Twenty minutes until the mountain pass. Twenty minutes to rewrite a prince’s legacy.

### 22. Mirage Convoy
The Atlas Mountains carved Morocco into jagged ribbons. Hunt rode in the second Hilux of the Coalition column, headlights killed, NVGs glowing green. Rain lashed the cliffs, turning switchbacks into slick slides. Ahead, the Cedar Crown convoy crawled—three MAN trucks, two armored SUVs, and a decoy flatbed carrying humanitarian supplies. Moroccan gendarmerie escorted them, unaware they protected a nuclear ransom.

Leyla drove, jaw set. “Sugar gel triggers in four minutes,” she said. “Once I hit it, engines seize for sixty seconds. After that, they limp but move.”

“We make sixty seconds count,” Hunt replied. He activated the remote. The lead truck coughed, spewed steam, and rolled to a stop blocking the pass. Drivers jumped down cursing. Coalition operatives in gendarmerie uniforms waved traffic to a halt, citing “rockfall.” Noura and Elena set up portable scanners while Barakat’s team established a faux command post.

Hunt moved fast. He and Leyla pried open the container’s locking bars, slicing the RFID seal and replacing it with an identical one embedded with a beacon. Inside, the reactor core glowed faint blue under LED strips. He taped micro-thermite charges to control rods—not to detonate now, but to render the core useless if Maverick recaptured it.

A shot cracked. Bullets sparked off the Hilux. Real gendarmerie arriving. The op’s clock evaporated. “FIDO—forget it, drive on,” Leyla growled, triggering the gel’s second pulse to buy another half-minute of stalled engines.

“VolgaShield column in our rear-view,” Barakat warned. “PMC SUVs with Russian plates. They’re spoofing humanitarian credentials.”
“SUSFU until we pry that core loose,” Hunt replied. “Situation unchanged, still fouled up, so we make the next move ugly for them.”

Leyla slammed the container door. “We need to move the whole box.”

“Too heavy,” Hunt said. “We steal the guts.” He cut the transport straps, signaled to DGST commandos. Together they slid the core onto a hover pallet Leyla borrowed from a Renault concept lab. The pallet hummed, magnets repelling the road surface. They guided it toward a waiting tunnel carved into the cliff—DGST’s hidden culvert.

VolgaShield fighters opened up with PKMs, tracers carving orange arcs. Coalition volunteers returned fire from behind basalt outcrops. Noura crouched over the hover pallet, shielding the core with her body as sparks ricocheted. “Please don’t shoot the scientist,” she yelled.

Hunt lobbed smoke grenades, turning the pass into a whiteout. He and Leyla shoved the pallet into the tunnel just as VolgaShield breached the roadblock. One mercenary sprinted forward, only to hit the hidden pitfall Barakat’s team dug hours earlier. He disappeared with a yelp.

“Core secure,” Leyla radioed. “We’re sealing the culvert.”

Hunt triggered the sugar gel failsafe, causing the stricken trucks to belch flames. The road became impassable. “Markarian’s SNAFU just hit high gear,” he muttered, tossing a last thermite puck under the decoy flatbed to ensure Cedar Crown lost both container and cover story.
Leyla sniffed. “If the real gendarmes pile on, this goes FUMTU in a heartbeat.”
“Then we keep it moving,” Hunt said. “FIDO.”

Sirens wailed down the mountain. Moroccan helicopters thumped overhead, spotlighting the chaos. Hunt signaled retreat. Coalition vehicles peeled away, blending into civilian traffic within minutes. They rendezvoused in an abandoned kasbah overlooking the desert. Inside, Noura checked the core, verifying the control rods were intact and marked for Coalition custody.

“Phase Sahara just lost its heart,” she said, relief washing over her face.

“Not yet,” Hunt cautioned. “Markarian will have backups. This buys us time. Use it.”

Leyla collapsed onto a crate, inhaled deeply, then looked up at Hunt with a tired grin. “You realize we just stole a reactor on a hover pallet in a thunderstorm?”

“Add it to the memoir,” Hunt said. He opened the Cedar Coalition portal on his tablet. Messages flooded in—Moroccan villagers offering shelter, Algerian truckers volunteering to report suspicious loads, even a Mossad analyst sending coordinates of Gwadar warehouses. The network was working.

Outside, lightning strobed over the desert. Hunt stepped into the rain, letting it wash the powder residue from his skin. Markarian would adjust, Maverick would retaliate, and Phase Sahara would mutate again. But tonight, the Cedar Coalition held a reactor core, a data trail, and a growing movement. He allowed himself a rare, thin smile before heading back inside to plan the next strike.

### 23. Nouakchott Signal
The Cedar Coalition’s first “field office” in Nouakchott looked like a sandblasted internet café wedged between a tire shop and a tea stall. Inside, Yara’s mesh routers blinked from a rack fashioned out of shipping pallets, while Mauritanian volunteers strung fiber lines along the ceiling like party lights. The stolen reactor core sat inside a refrigerated truck out back, guarded by DGST commandos disguised as fishmongers.

Hunt leaned over a folding table covered in radios and paper maps. Amina Haddad briefed the joint cell. “Levon Markarian just landed at Nouakchott’s military apron using a diplomatic waver,” she said. “He’s hosting a midnight ‘philanthropy dinner’ at the port cannery. Translation: he’s moving backup control rods onto a Liberian-flag freighter. Our job is to air the Cedar Coalition portal live, show Mauritania the evidence, and raid the cannery before Markarian’s plane wheels up.” Hunt chuckled without humor. “Classic FISH—fighting in someone else’s house. Let’s make sure the locals see we’re here to help, not wreck their port.”

Leyla handed out burner tablets preloaded with the Coalition app. “The portal pushes real-time heat maps, convoy telemetry, and a whistleblower form in Arabic, French, and Pulaar,” she said. “Noura coded a failsafe: if Markarian tries to hijack the stream, the entire dataset mirrors to Interpol, Al Jazeera, and the Moroccan royal court simultaneously.”

Noura crouched beside the reactor core, tapping readings into her tablet. “We also go public with this tonight,” she said. “Transparency is our armor. Once people see how Cedar Crown tried to hide a hospital reactor inside a ‘filtration’ shipment, they’ll stop believing Maverick’s press releases.”

Barakat adjusted his Mauritanian gendarmerie uniform. “Local colonel agreed to look the other way if we keep civilian casualties at zero,” he said. “Also, he wants a Cedar Coalition badge for his daughter. Apparently we’re a brand now.”

Hunt checked his rifle, then the Saint Barbara icon. He felt the eyes of the volunteers—students, truckers, fishermen—on him. “Portal goes live when we seize the cannery’s uplink,” he said. “Elena, you and Amina handle the legal broadcast. Leyla runs the on-site camera. Barakat and I grab Markarian. Noura and Yara keep the core cold and the data hot. Questions?”

A young volunteer raised a hand. “What if Maverick hijacks the feed again?”

“Then we hijack it back faster,” Hunt said. Laughter rippled, tension easing.

They rolled out at dusk. Nouakchott’s port smelled of fish and diesel. The cannery’s floodlights washed the dunes in sterile white. Markarian’s guests arrived in Land Cruisers, their drivers flashing diplomatic badges. Hunt’s team infiltrated among catering staff. Leyla carried a camera rig disguised as a wine cart. Elena wore a headscarf and toted a stack of “environmental compliance forms” that doubled as warrants.

Inside, Markarian toasted “regional stability” while VolgaShield guards watched the exits. Hunt ghosted along the catwalks above, planting RF beacons on the crates labeled **Nutritional Supplements**. His earbud crackled—Yara’s voice. “Portal servers synced. Just need your uplink.”

Hunt clipped a fiber tap into the cannery’s backbone and keyed his throat mic. “Upload in three, two…” Screens around the dining hall flickered. The Cedar Coalition logo bloomed—cedar tree wrapped around a reactor schematic. Footage from Casablanca, Limassol, and the Atlas pass stitched together, narrated by Leyla live from the floor. “This is the Cedar Coalition,” she said in French and Arabic. “We intercepted a nuclear extortion plot funded by foreign princes. Tonight we show you how.”

Guests gaped. Phones came out. Some filmed, others dialed handlers. Markarian’s smile thinned. He motioned to his guards. Barakat cut them off, flashing gendarmerie credentials. Amina strode in alongside Mauritanian police, brandishing warrants. Elena marched up to Markarian and served him papers with theatrical flourish.

“You’re making a mistake,” Markarian hissed.

“So did Talal,” Hunt said, appearing behind him. He cuffed Markarian while Leyla’s camera streamed the moment to the portal and, thanks to Noura’s failsafe, to every major newsroom subscribed to the Coalition feed. Outside, Mauritanian crowds gathered, watching on borrowed phones.

VolgaShield’s extraction team tried to rush the side gate. Yara tripped the port’s smart lights, plunging the yard into darkness except for the path Coalition volunteers wanted them to take—straight into a stack of shipping containers Barakat rigged with flashbang charges. Explosions popped; the mercs staggered. DGST commandos swarmed, zip-tying wrists.

“Signal is peaking,” Noura reported from the safehouse. “Fifty thousand viewers. Portal reporting twenty new whistleblower tips already. One is from Gwadar. Another from Oman. We opened the firehose.”

Hunt yanked Markarian toward the exit. “Tell Maverick,” he said quietly, “that the Cedar Coalition doesn’t do ghosts. We do broadcasts.” Markarian just glared.

Back at the safehouse, they stowed the core in a climate-controlled box and started prepping an intercontinental courier flight. Maya called, voice vibrating with adrenaline. “Half of West Africa just joined your portal,” she said. “You toppled a money man and crowd-sourced four new leads in six minutes. Enjoy the fame while it lasts.”

“Fame gets us tips,” Hunt replied. “Tips get us reactors. Reactors get us sleep.” He doubted the last part, but saying it out loud made the exhaustion feel purposeful.

### 24. Gwadar Ghosts
The Pakistani coast smelled of diesel, cardamom tea, and ozone from the cranes dotting Gwadar’s deep-water port. Hunt stood beneath a darkened gantry, night-vision goggles painting everything in ghost greens. Beside him, Mossad officer Yael Ben-Ami adjusted her suppressed Tavor. “Funny seeing you on my continent,” she whispered.

“Your double agent started this mess,” Hunt said. “You’re helping clean it.”

Gwadar’s special economic zone buzzed with Chinese contractors and Pakistani marines. Under one hangar, a consortium called Baloch Gulf Logistics stored “water treatment modules” identical to Cedar Crown crates. The Cedar Coalition portal lit up with reports from stevedores who’d watched “foreign engineers” load glowing cylinders into refrigerated containers. The shipments were due to sail east toward Karachi at dawn.

Leyla and Elena set up a fake WHO inspection team at the gate, complete with laminated IDs and a van filled with empty sample coolers. Yara spoofed the port’s CCTV feed to hide their approach. Noura, still in Nouakchott hours earlier, piped in via AR glasses, overlaying heat signatures on Hunt’s HUD. “Fourth container from the east wall,” she said. “Radiation spike at 0.7 millisievert. Coolant pumps humming.”

Yael knelt, splicing into the fiber trunk connecting the hangar to a Chinese-operated data hub. “If I yank this, Beijing will call Islamabad in thirty seconds,” she warned.

“Then yank it when we’re already inside,” Hunt said. He signaled Barakat, who now wore a borrowed Pakistani Rangers uniform, and DGST officer Amina, who had flown in under Omani diplomatic cover. The coalition moved like a single organism now; Hunt took quiet pride in that.

They breached at 0200. Hunt and Yael climbed a service ladder, traversed ceiling beams, and dropped behind the guards. Leyla rolled in with the WHO van, flashing bogus paperwork about “contaminated saline.” While Elena argued in Urdu about “public health,” Hunt slipped to the target container, cut the seal, and eased the door open. Inside, stabilizer chains pinned a micro-core identical to the one they’d nabbed in Morocco—only this one carried an engraved plate: **Project Atlas – Karachi Node**.

“They’re naming phases after our moves,” Leyla muttered over comms. “Ego much?”

Hunt rigged the control rods with trackers and thermite, then called for extraction. Before they could roll the core out, headlights flared—Pakistani marines, tipped off by a Chinese security officer who’d noticed Yara’s camera loop. Barakat and Amina intercepted them with a flurry of official stamps and UN letters, buying minutes but not hours.

“Plan B,” Yael said. She pulled a collapsible drone out of her pack, launched it straight up. It disgorged flechettes onto the hangar’s power lines, plunging the bay into darkness. Hunt used the black-out to swap the reactor core with a decoy Noura designed—identical mass but filled with inert polymer. They wheeled the real core out the rear cargo door into a waiting refrigerated truck painted with Karachi food bank logos.

Sirens wailed. Hunt keyed his mic. “Portal push in five. Make it loud.”

Yara went live from a secure server in Amman, broadcasting thermal footage of the Gwadar swap alongside documents linking the hangar to a shell company Markarian registered in Muscat. Pakistani social media erupted. Within minutes, Islamabad’s Ministry of Interior announced a surprise “joint inspection” of the zone. International pressure forced the marines to stand down long enough for Hunt’s convoy to slip through a service gate escorted by a sympathetic colonel.

As dawn broke over the Arabian Sea, the Cedar Coalition truck rolled onto a chartered Omani ferry. Noura met them in Muscat with a portable lab. “Two cores down,” she said. “One to dismantle, one to weaponize against propaganda.”

Yael clasped Hunt’s forearm. “Samir’s ghosts keep haunting us,” she said softly. “But tonight, we exorcised one.”

Hunt watched the Gwadar cranes recede. “Markarian will spin this,” he said. “Let him try. We own the narrative now.”

### 25. Azraq Line
The dhow *Azraq* cut through the Arabian Sea under a sliver moon, its teak hull creaking as if it remembered every smuggling run since the Silk Road. Leyla’s tracker, embedded weeks earlier in a crate’s drainage plug, pulsed steady. Hunt watched the blinking dot on his tablet from a CIA “borrowed” P-8 Poseidon flying lazy S-turns 10,000 feet above. Beside him, Maya’s holographic avatar flickered, projected from a kneeboard slate.

“The dhow’s captain registered as a Red Crescent relief vessel,” Maya said. “Pakistani intelligence flagged it once for hauling cheap Iranian fuel. Now it’s hauling a micro-core scaled for urban blackouts. Markarian calls it Phase Azraq—flood the Gulf with fear, force Oman to pay Cedar Crown for ‘grid insurance.’”

Leyla and Yael sat two rows back, strapping into HALO rigs. “We’re dropping over water?” Yael asked. “I thought you hated the sea.”

“I hate losing reactors more,” Hunt said. He checked his gear: rebreather, fins, suppressed MPX, sugar gel ampoules, an EMP grenade Leyla built out of drone parts, and Saint Barbara still taped inside his plate carrier. The plan was maddening: jump with only four operators (Hunt, Leyla, Yael, Barakat), land near the dhow, board via hook-and-climb, seize the core, stream the takedown through the Cedar Coalition portal, and sink the hull if necessary. All before sunrise and without sparking a naval incident between Oman, Iran, and Pakistan.

*Illustration: HALO insertion & Azraq deck plan (jump altitude, target lane).*

“Coalition portal servers ready,” Yara radioed from Muscat. “New feature: real-time geofenced updates. Gulf fishermen can tag suspicious ships. BLUF: if it sails, we see it. If it goes FAFO, we tag it.”

Noura chimed in from the lab. “Remember, this core’s coolant mix uses lithium salts. If the casing fractures, seawater sets off a toxic cloud. Do not shoot it unless you want glowing tuna.”

“Copy,” Hunt said dryly. He fitted his rebreather, slid toward the drop ramp. The Poseidon’s bay door yawned open, revealing moonlit swells below. Wind tore at his rigging. He clapped Leyla’s shoulder. “On three,” he said through the mic. “One. Two.” They jumped.

Air seized him, then the parachute jerked. He steered toward the dark blot that was the *Azraq*, ditched the chute just above the waves, and plunged into the sea. Cold punched his ribs. He swam slow to avoid phosphorescence, popped up beneath the dhow’s shadow, and placed a magnetic grapnel. Leyla climbed first, fluid and silent. Hunt followed, boot soles finding worn wood.

Deck lights glowed amber. The crew—half Pakistani, half hired mercenaries—played cards near the bow. Leyla lobbed a flashbang disguised as a tea flask. It detonated quietly, a magnesium pulse that left the crew reeling. Hunt swept them with the MPX but fired only warning shots. Barakat cuffed them while Yael secured the wheelhouse.

Hunt bolted for the cargo hold. Inside, the crate he’d tracked for months sat lashed to the keel, coolant pumps humming. He pried it open. The core gleamed, smaller than the others—designed for a skyscraper’s sub-basement or a desalination plant. He wired thermite failsafes, then attached a live-stream camera Leyla had disguised as an inspection light.

“Portal live in three,” Leyla said. The Cedar Coalition logo flooded the dhow’s deck via a portable holo projector. Hunt addressed the camera. “Phase Azraq neutralized,” he announced. “No more ghost ships holding cities hostage.” Within seconds, Coalition volunteers, Gulf fishermen, and journalists tuned in. Comments flooded the feed.

Suddenly, mortars thumped. Shells splashed around the dhow, geysers of water drenching the deck. On the horizon, IRGC fast boats tore across the waves, their crews apparently tipped that someone hijacked their “aid” vessel. Hunt grabbed the EMP grenade, tossed it into the sea ahead of the boats. It detonated underwater, frying their radar. Boats veered wildly. One slammed into the *Azraq’s* stern, rocking the core.

“We can’t hold the hull,” Yael shouted. “Either sink it or tow it.”

Hunt triggered the sugar gel he’d planted earlier in the dhow’s engine intake. The engine seized, stall warning wailing. He keyed his mic. “Azraq scuttled. Core secured. Need extraction vector now.”

Maya replied. “Omani SAR helo inbound. But IRGC radio chatter says they’ll board in six minutes with rockets. Suggest you jump with the core.”

Leyla cursed. “You want us to HALO in and free-fall out? With a reactor?”

Hunt looked at the hover pallet they’d brought folded in a duffel. “Not free-fall,” he said. “Surf.” They unfolded the pallet, strapped the core down, and shoved it toward the stern. Hunt attached parachute risers to the pallet’s corners. “When the helo arrives, we hook this and ride it skyward.”

“Reacher never did this,” Barakat muttered.

“Reacher never had portals,” Hunt said. The Omani helo roared overhead, winch cable dangling. Hunt clipped the pallet, signaled thumbs-up, and held on as the core lifted. The world blurred—spray, rockets, gunfire. Then they were airborne, dangling beneath the helo, the Cedar Coalition camera still streaming as the dhow burned below. The portal exploded with viewers.

Back on dry land, Oman quietly announced a joint Cedar Coalition–DGST custody arrangement. Noura ran diagnostics, declaring the Azraq core intact. Maya flashed a rare smile over holo. “Phase Azraq officially dead,” she said. “Also, half the Gulf just subscribed to your portal. Markarian can’t hide anywhere.”

Hunt stripped off his drenched gear, muscles trembling. “He’ll try,” he said. “So we keep running louder.”

#### Interlude 08 – Markarian’s Field Order
**Secure Channel: L. Markarian → Maverick**  
**Timestamp:** 2300Z  

- Talal neutralized. Cedar Coalition now broadcast-capable. Portal viewership surged across Maghreb.  
- Phase Sahara primary core captured. Remaining assets: two guidance trees staged Muscat/Gwadar, one micro-core aboard dhow *Azraq*.  
- Recommend pivot: leak false intel about Casablanca success to bait Hunt into Gulf of Oman while Phase Atlas (Europe) matures.  
- Request reinforcement: cyber team to infiltrate Cedar Coalition portal; inject disinformation about “rogue humanitarian” faction to fracture public support.

**Status:** Awaiting instructions.  
—Markarian

> **@SeaLaneWatcher:** Uploading tug schedules Muscat–Karachi. Use for next intercept.  
> **@MedTechOman:** Cedar Coalition portal is crashing under load. Need mirror servers? Can host in Salalah clinic.

> **@BalochFisher:** Saw foreign yacht *Azraq* off Pasni, heavy escort. Sending GPS now.  
> **HuntLineOps:** Got it. Next waypoint locked. Stay safe. We’re not done.

### 26. Atlas Relay
DEVGRU’s Forward Element Bravo occupied an abandoned NATO radar station in Crete, its satellite dishes now tuned to Cedar Coalition frequencies. Hunt arrived via Osprey, boots hitting the tarmac beside operators in gray plate carriers marked only with blood types. Chief Rhodes, the DEVGRU liaison who owed Hunt from a Yemen dust-up, greeted him with a curt nod. “You asked for ISR and muscle,” Rhodes said. “SOCOM gave you a task unit with limited ROE. Target?”

Hunt unrolled a map. “Phase Atlas is rebooting through a bank of European data centers masquerading as tsunami warning relays. Markarian’s coder, Leyland, moved the Cedar Crown kill-switch into a colocation facility outside Thessaloniki. If he flips it, every Coalition stream goes dark. We have to yank the drives, keep him breathing long enough to trace his back-end, and broadcast the takedown to prove transparency.”

Leyla tapped a tablet displaying ISR feeds from MQ-9 Reapers orbiting overhead. “Security stack: private G4S guards, Hellenic Police liaison, and two VolgaShield shooters. Rhodes, your boys breach from roof via fast rope. Hunt and I take the server spine. Noura remotes in from Muscat to clone the drives. Barakat runs QRF in Hellenic uniforms.”

Rhodes grinned. “Simple smash-and-grab with cyber garnish. Love it.” He flipped his helmet face shield down. “DEVGRU callsign for this run is TRIDENT. JTAC on station with V-22 for exfil. ROE: non-lethal preferred unless hostile meets lethal force criteria.”

Night fell. The assault force inserted via MH-60 stealth hawks skimming wave tops. Hunt and Leyla rappelled onto the rooftop, clipped into a fiber junction box, and injected a Cedar Coalition beacon that hijacked the facility’s security feeds. Downstairs, Markarian’s coder Leyland monitored screens, unaware that DEVGRU operators were ghosting through the stairwells using suppressed HK416s.

“JTAC, TRIDENT-1,” Rhodes whispered into his throat mic. “Set ISR overwatch. Thermal shows two tangos in Server Row Bravo.” The JTAC acknowledged, piping live feeds to Hunt’s HUD. Hunt moved through cold aisles, MPX up, while Leyla patched into the backbone. She whispered acronyms like a litany—“BGP reroute, SSL pinning, SOC alert suppression”—while Noura’s voice crackled over encrypted satcom detailing which racks housed Cedar Crown’s remote kill chain.

They breached the secure cage with a carbon screwdriver. Inside, Leyland spun around, eyes wide. “You can’t—” he began. Hunt slammed him against the rack, zip-tied wrists, and shoved a mic in his face. “Cedar Coalition broadcast live,” Leyla announced. “Here’s Leyland, architect of Phase Atlas, caught wiring nuclear blackmail into a fake tsunami relay.” Comments flooded the portal feed as civilians watched the raid in real time.

VolgaShield shooters stormed the aisle. DEVGRU operators fired controlled double taps, dropping them before stray rounds could ping off server blades. Rhodes tossed Hunt a drive case. “Pop the blades, Sam.” Hunt yanked three chassis, slotted them into the case, and handed them to Leyla. She transmitted encrypted copies to Yara and Maya simultaneously. “Atlas kill-switch cloned,” Noura confirmed. “I’m rerouting it through Coalition infrastructure so any attempt to trigger Cedar Crown now pings us first.”

Greek police sirens wailed outside. Barakat’s QRF blocked the gate with a marked cruiser, citing “cyber emergency.” Rhodes signaled exfil. The team bugged the racks with nanocams, injected false uptime metrics to delay detection, and withdrew through the roof, hauling Leyland with them. As the MH-60 lifted, Leyla overlaid the live feed with acronyms the Coalition chat loved—DEVGRU, JTAC, ISR, QRF—explaining in plain language how a multinational task unit just neutralized Phase Atlas without firing on civilians.

Back at the Crete relay, Rhodes clasped Hunt’s forearm. “Next time you want to parade DEVGRU on a livestream, give me more than twelve hours’ notice,” he said.

“Couldn’t risk leaks,” Hunt replied. “Besides, the world seeing Tier One operators backing a transparency op sends a message.”

Leyla checked the portal metrics. “Viewership spiked across Europe,” she said. “Students in Portugal just formed a Cedar Coalition chapter. Markarian’s bots are trying to label us a CIA front, but we’re winning the narrative.”

Hunt looked toward the horizon, where thunderstorms lit the Aegean. “Phase Atlas is crippled,” he said quietly. “Phase Sahara’s core sits in Coalition custody. Markarian’s running out of chess pieces.”

“He still has influence,” Noura warned over comms. “Don’t get cocky.”

“I don’t do cocky,” Hunt replied. “I do relentless.” He turned to Rhodes. “Thanks for the kit. Tell SOCOM their acronym made the portal trend.”

Rhodes laughed. “DEVGRU trending. Never thought I’d live to see it.”

### 27. Signal Mirror
The Cedar Coalition’s forward node in Muscat was a repurposed Omani SAR hangar packed with Pelican cases and a dozen national flags tacked to the wall. Hunt stood beside a digital sand table while Maya’s holo hovered overhead. “BLUF,” she said, “Phase Sahara’s convoy leaves Casablanca in nineteen hours. DGST has eyes on the road, but Markarian still controls the narrative in Europe. We need a mirror strike—one that shows every ally that the Coalition and SOCOM are shoulder to shoulder.”

Chief Rhodes from DEVGRU, Barakat in borrowed Royal Army fatigues, Amina Haddad, and Leyla crowded around. A FRAGO from SOCOM blinked on the screen, authorizing a joint QRF composed of DEVGRU shooters, DGST commandos, and Cedar Coalition liaisons. “We insert via C-130 to Nouasseur Air Base, roll under Moroccan command, and stage along the Atlas foothills,” Hunt summed up. “Leyla runs the info ops, Noura remotes the telemetry, Rhodes handles the door-kickers. SITREP every thirty minutes.”

*Illustration: Signal Mirror sand table (DEVGRU TRIDENT lanes, QRF positions, ISR orbits).*

“What about morale?” Leyla asked. “Team’s FIGMO. Half of them already packed for whatever comes after Morocco.”

“BOHICA,” Rhodes said dryly. “Every time we think this saga ends, Markarian drops another shell game. They’ll suck it up.”

Barakat pointed at the sand table. “DGST reports VolgaShield’s escort is down to a single armored BTR and two pickups. If we crack the BTR with a FAFO warning shot, the rest will fold.”

Yael Ben-Ami patched in from Tel Aviv, her video feed jittering. “Mossad has a HUMINT string on Leyland’s backups,” she said. “If you snag them, Israel will front a public statement supporting the Cedar Coalition. That defangs the ‘CIA front’ accusations.”

“Tell our Jerusalem rare-weapons restorer he owes me a favor if we pull this off,” Hunt quipped. “Maybe that Aleppo Madonna we rescued together.”

Noura’s voice came through a speaker. “Remember: the portal is more than propaganda now. We have sixty thousand subscribers expecting honesty. Stream the raid like we did with Atlas. If it gets FUBAR, you tell them in real time.”

“Understood,” Hunt said. He looked around the hangar. Exhaustion hung over everyone, but the BOHICA joke had them grinning again. “Rhodes, get your JTAC synced with our ISR Reapers. Leyla, preload the stream with a BLUF for Moroccan viewers. Barakat, spin up two QRF SUVs under Moroccan plates. We wheels-up in sixty minutes.”

As the briefing broke, Maya lingered on the holo. “Last note,” she said. “Washington wants a clean paper trail. No more plausible deniability. The President just referenced the Cedar Coalition in a speech. Congratulations—you’re official.”

“Great,” Hunt muttered. “Now when things go SNAFU, Congress gets popcorn.”

Leyla shoved a travel mug into his hand. “Drink. Then go remind Markarian why FAFO is more than a meme.”

They boarded the C-130 just before dawn. The cabin smelled of hydraulic fluid and instant coffee. Hunt strapped in beside Rhodes, checked his MPX, and scrawled a final entry in his notebook: “Phase Sahara—mirror strike—no ghosts.” The aircraft rumbled down the runway, lifting toward Morocco. Somewhere ahead, Markarian assumed the Cedar Coalition would tire out. Hunt intended to prove otherwise.

#### Interlude 10 – SOCOM SITREP (Extract)
> **From:** JSOC Watch Floor  
> **To:** SOCOM, DGST, Cedar Coalition  
> **Subject:** SITREP 27-Alpha (Atlas Relay/Azraq follow-on)  
> **1.** Atlas kill-switch drives secured; Leyland in custody. DEVGRU/TRIDENT exfil RTB Crete.  
> **2.** Azraq core transferred to Coalition/DGST custody. Omani SAR logs reflect “joint environmental drill.”  
> **3.** Phase Sahara convoy confirmed departing Casablanca 1900Z; escort reduced. DGST requests Coalition live-stream to manage INFOSEC.  
> **4.** ROE: Non-lethal preferred until VolgaShield initiates TIC. JTAC callsign TOGA on station with MQ-9 for ISR.  
> **5.** Coalition portal viewership spike +23%; FAFO messaging resonating with Gulf fishermen. Maintain transparency to avoid BOHICA-level morale dips.  
> **6.** Risk of JANFU (“Joint Army-Navy Fouled Up”) if EUCOM liaisons inject alternate ROE mid-mission. Recommend single command channel.  
> **End SITREP.**

### 28. Atlas Gate
Nouasseur Air Base buzzed with DGST troopers, Moroccan gendarmes, and Cedar Coalition volunteers in borrowed uniforms. Hunt, Rhodes, Barakat, Leyla, and Amina stood in a hangar turned staging bay while a BLUF scrolled across a holowall: **Convoy ETA 0400Z. Objective: capture core, stream arrest, zero civilian casualties.** Rhodes briefed the DEVGRU assault teams, using a pointer to sketch the approach along a series of switchbacks labeled with call signs.

“We’re splitting TRIDENT into two elements,” Rhodes said. “Team One fast-ropes onto the BTR and hits it with a non-lethal EMP lance. Team Two handles the pickups. Moroccan QRF seals the rear. Cedar Coalition handles INFOSEC. If this turns TARFU, we fall back to DGST checkpoint Delta and go FIGMO in the morning.”

Amina smirked. “We don’t get FIGMO here. Markarian keeps resetting the clock.”

Leyla adjusted her tactical headset. “Portal stream is preloaded with a BOHICA disclaimer,” she told Hunt. “Viewers know we might have to cut feed if the convoy drivers go FAFO.”

“Good,” Hunt said. “Noura, you in position?”

Noura’s voice came through a satlink. “Yes. I have two radiation drones ready to hover above the convoy. If the core takes damage, I’ll call FISH and you’ll scrub the stream.”

Maya pinged a FRAGO to Hunt’s tablet. “SITREP: French media already sniffing around. Once you seize the core, Rhodes’ JTAC will trigger a BLUF broadcast in French, Arabic, and English. Keep it sharp.”

They rolled north in a convoy of DGST SUVs and armored pickups. The Atlas Mountains loomed dark, rain slashing across the headlights. ISR drones painted the road in thermal colors, each vehicle tagged with call signs. When the Cedar Crown convoy appeared—one BTR, two pickups, a refrigerated truck—Rhodes whispered, “TRIDENT, set TIC posture. JTAC, confirm airspace cold.” The JTAC responded with a calm “Clear.”

The ambush unfolded with brutal precision. DEVGRU operators dropped onto the BTR, jamming an EMP lance into the turret. Sparks flew. The BTR shuddered, losing power. Moroccan gendarmes boxed in the pickups. Hunt and Leyla sprinted for the reefer truck, bolts cutters in hand. Barakat’s QRF swarmed the rear, hauling VolgaShield shooters out by their collars. Leyla snapped a live camera onto the truck door just as Hunt cracked it open. The Cedar Coalition stream lit up with comments: **FAFO warning issued.** **QRF looks sharp.**

*Illustration: Atlas Gate ambush diagram (BTR landing point, QRF arcs, drone lanes).*

Inside the truck, the core sat in a shock-mounted cradle. Hunt taped thermite bricks to the control rods. “SNAFU insurance,” he muttered. Leyla translated for the viewers, explaining why the charges ensured the core couldn’t be weaponized. Outside, two VolgaShield fighters tried to bolt into the scrub. Rhodes shouted “FAFO!” The DEVGRU team fired warning shots. The fighters dropped their weapons, hands up.

“Portal feed peaking at 90k,” Yara reported. “European audiences are glued. Keep the SITREP flowing.”

Hunt pulled Markarian’s courier—a blue-suited fixer named Saeed—out of the BTR and shoved him in front of Leyla’s camera. “Tell the world what you were hauling,” Leyla demanded. Saeed sweated, glancing at the rifles. “Medical filtration,” he stammered. Hunt leaned in. “Lie again and I broadcast your offshore account list.” Saeed swallowed, then confessed on stream, detailing Talal’s crypto laundering and the Phase Sahara route. The comments exploded.

A distant rumble echoed through the mountains. “We’ve got a jeep forming a breakaway,” Amina warned. “Probably trying to go FIGMO.”

“JTAC, request FIDO drone strike on the breakaway vehicle,” Rhodes said. The JTAC cleared a Reaper to fire a Hellfire into the jeep’s path, creating a crater that forced it to stop without casualties. The portal stream caught the explosion, comments filling with **FAFO** memes and **SITREP** shout-outs.

Within ten minutes, Moroccan media arrived. Amina gave a statement in Arabic, citing the Cedar Coalition and DGST as a unified front. Hunt closed the stream with a BLUF: “Core secure. No civilians hurt. Phase Sahara is now a case study, not a threat.” He looked at the camera. “Markarian, every time you try to BOHICA us, we throw a FAFO back.”

### 29. BOHICA Ridge
The convoy survivors—and the Cedar Crown core—rolled into DGST’s hardened depot outside Fez as dawn painted the Atlas pink. Hunt stood on a catwalk overlooking the hangar where the reactor sat under guard. Rhodes, Barakat, Leyla, Noura, and Maya (via holo) gathered around a digital sand table labeled **BOHICA RIDGE**—the Coalition nickname for the point where Markarian kept forcing them to “bend over, here it comes again.”

“SITREP,” Maya began. “Phase Sahara’s core secure. VolgaShield detainees processed, Saeed singing like a canary. Riyadh issued a BOHICA statement about ‘rogue princes.’ However, Phase Atlas backdoors are still live in Italy and Spain. Leyland’s servers gave us IPs, but we need to neutralize them before Markarian’s Blue Falcon allies reboot the network.”

*Illustration: BOHICA Ridge briefing board (Fez depot, Atlas relay nodes, remaining targets).*

Leyla smirked. “Blue Falcons everywhere. They sold out their own cities for crypto.”

Rhodes pointed at the map. “DEVGRU is rotating home. FIGMO status for my shooters. But we’ll leave a JSOC liaison in Fez. You’ve got Moroccan SOF, DGST, and Cedar Coalition muscle. Use them. BLUF: keep streaming honesty, keep the populace on your side.”

Noura checked the core’s readouts. “Radiation stable. Once we install it in a Cedar Coalition lab, we can reverse-engineer every crooked reactor out there. But we still have to dismantle the Atlas backdoors. If Markarian flicks one switch, your portal goes dark. FUBAR city.”

Barakat radioed from the gate. “JTAC reports a drone—unknown origin—circling BOHICA Ridge. Probably a FAFO attempt. DGST scrambled fighters.”

“Let them,” Hunt said. “We need the world to see that every time Markarian tries FAFO, the Coalition has redundancy.”

Amina walked in holding a USB drive. “Moroccan parliament wants a BLUF for the public,” she said. “Tell them what just happened. Keep it under two minutes. Our people are watching.”

Hunt nodded. “And we’ll give them one more twist,” he said. “We turn BOHICA Ridge into a livestreamed press conference where we ask the hard question: why did Cedar Crown ever get this far? We answer it ourselves—because we let ghosts run the world. That ends today.”

He looked at Leyla and Noura. “Prep the feed. Barakat, QRF on alert. Rhodes, take your FIGMO boys home; tell them FAFO memes are trending.”

Rhodes laughed. “Never thought I’d brief a mission with FAFO trending. SOCOM’s going to love that.”
