### Epilogue – Cedar Crown Lives
Casablanca’s port at midnight was all sodium lamps and crane silhouettes. A teak-hulled yacht named *Raheeq* idled beside Warehouse Delta-12, its deck lined with tourists who thought they’d booked an exclusive cruise. Below deck, workers rolled a container stamped **Industrial Filtration Units** into the hold. Inside, just as Maya warned, sat a modular reactor core wrapped in desiccant and carbon foam. Levon Markarian—Maverick’s mentor and the architect of Phase Sahara—stood beside it, clipboard in hand, linen suit immaculate.

Maverick strode down the gangway, fresh bandage across his jaw. “Talal is in a gilded cage,” he said quietly. “Riyadh bleeds credibility. We sail east as planned.”

Markarian tapped the container. “Phase Sahara moves tonight across the Atlas, then south to Nouakchott. Hunt is alive. He’ll follow the tracker you planted on purpose.”

Maverick smiled. “Exactly. Let him chase. Every time he bites, we learn his rhythm.” He dialed a satphone. “Initiate Phase Sahara. Morocco to Mauritania. Deploy the decoy convoy with overt security. Our real core travels under a refugee shipment.”

He didn’t notice the coin-sized tracker Leyla had embedded inside the container’s drainage plug when she “accidentally” brushed against it hours earlier, casing the dock as a stevedore. Nor did he notice the microdrone perched on the mast humming data back to Beirut. In a safe house overlooking the Corniche, Hunt watched the blip crawl across his laptop map and allowed himself a rare smile.

“Run,” he whispered. “We’re already ahead.”

---

### Preview – Book Two: Aegean Lock
Six weeks later, Hunt stood beneath the Parthenon’s floodlit columns, Athens humming far below like a generator. The Cedar Coalition had gone fully public—thirty-two partner clinics online, seven reactors dismantled, one Saudi prince in gilded timeout. None of it stopped the encrypted ping that lit Hunt’s phone: **AEGEAN LOCK BREACH**.

Yael Ben-Ami arrived first, raindrops beading on her leather jacket. “Greek Coast Guard seized a trawler stuffed with cobalt rods,” she said without preamble. “Manifest ties back to Markarian’s old mentor. Guess who owns the warehouse in Thessaloniki where they planned to mill the rods? Your Jerusalem rare-weapons restorer friend. Apparently somebody hijacked his safehouse circuit.”

Leyla unfolded a new dossier labeled **Operation Aegean Lock**. Photos of Mykonos marinas, Crete radar masts, and Meteora monasteries peppered the pages. “Talal’s crypto’s gone, so Cedar Crown sold shares to a Greek shipping clan,” she said. “They rebranded Phase Atlas as a boutique energy start-up promising blackout-proof microgrids across the islands. Investors lined up. So did smugglers. Tarek’s tip about Ioannis’ ‘restoration shop’ checks out—he’s laundering conflict diamonds to bankroll a European strike.”

Noura appeared on the holo from Muscat, lab coat streaked with cobalt dust. “The rods they seized are ‘dirty frequency’ hybrids,” she warned. “If someone installs them inside tourist hotels, the fallout reads like HVAC noise. We need eyes on every substation between Piraeus and the Cyclades.”

Hunt looked over the railing toward the Aegean. Ferries cut neon arcs across the black water. “Book Two starts here,” he said. “Same coalition. New coastline. Maverick thinks islands make better mazes than deserts. Let’s bring a bigger map.”

Barakat clapped him on the shoulder. “DEVGRU liaison says Rhodes—both the island and the SEAL—are on standby,” he joked. “And Ioannis’ warehouse is already on Mossad’s naughty list thanks to those diamonds.”

Leyla passed Hunt a fresh Saint Barbara icon she’d scavenged from a Greek chapel, its paint flaked and sea-salted. “New theater, same monsters,” she said. “You in?”

Hunt tucked the icon into his vest. “Always,” he replied. “Prep the mesh network. Wake the portal. Tell Athens to brace for ghosts.”

*Aegean Lock* launches the next arc: Greek maritime cat-and-mouse, Olympic tunnels repurposed as vaults, and a face-off with the shipping dynasty bankrolling Cedar Crown’s resurrection. The Cedar Coalition’s work isn’t over—it’s just headed for rougher seas.

## Book Two – Aegean Lock

### 30. Acropolis Hook
Athens by day smelled like souvlaki and exhaust; at night it smelled like ozone and history. Hunt leaned against the railing of the Acropolis Museum roof, scanning Syntagma’s traffic for the matte-black van his allies promised. The Saint Barbara icon Tarek slipped him glinted under the floodlights. Beside him, Leyla scrolled through Ioannis Petropoulos’ restoration invoices—half Renaissance icons, half “emergency courier fees” paid by a diamond co-op tied to Cedar Crown.

Yael Ben-Ami appeared, hair tucked beneath a courier cap. “Ioannis’ front just lit up my Mossad triggers,” she said. “His ‘restoration shipment’ arrived from Sierra Leone via Antwerp nine hours ago. Diamonds hidden inside brass censer molds, bound for the same shipping clan financing Maverick’s reboot.”

Leyla flipped a tablet toward Hunt. “Ioannis launders relics and rifle parts out of a warehouse in Ano Liosia, on the edge of Athens’ freight belt. Tonight he’s meeting a Cyclades shipping lawyer and the Uzbek fixer we dusted in Aqaba. Guess he didn’t drown in the mud.”

Barakat slid out of the promised van wearing a Hellenic Police windbreaker. “Athens Metro gave us five minutes of CCTV blind spots,” he said. “After that, Greek counterterror will show up and ask why Jordanians, Lebanese, and Israelis are raiding an icon restorer.”

“Then we finish in four,” Hunt said. He checked his MPX, now fitted with one of Brother Elias’ Steyr bolts. “Yara?”

“Mesh nodes planted on the trolley lines,” Yara replied over comms from the van. “Once you breach, I beam footage straight to the Coalition portal and—per Tarek’s request—to the Bedouin cooperative’s new dashboard. They funded this with that revenge-cell money. Might as well watch the return on investment.”

They rolled toward Ano Liosia in two vehicles, headlights killed, each wearing Greek municipal uniforms Leyla counterfeited. Ioannis’ warehouse sat wedged between a marble yard and a shadow market for metric tools. Through binoculars, Hunt saw forklifts moving crates stamped **ICON RESTORATION – FRAGILE**. He also saw the diamond crew—West African couriers, two Uzbek gunmen, and Ioannis himself—arguing in hushed Greek over a satchel stuffed with cash.

Leyla triggered the fire alarm from a municipal control box, sending sprinkler pipes hissing. As workers scrambled, Hunt and Yael slipped through a side door, suppressed weapons up. They moved past rows of partially restored icons—Saint Nicholas, John the Baptist, even a cracked Madonna. Beneath each painting lay disassembled weapon parts, waiting to be smuggled under the guise of art.

Ioannis rounded the corner, a gilded censer in hand. Hunt tackled him into a workbench while Yael disarmed the Uzbek fixer—the same man whose license plate they’d logged in Aqaba. “You cost me two trucks,” the fixer snarled. “You cost us a city,” Hunt shot back, zip-tying his wrists.

Leyla streamed the takedown live, narrating how diamond profits funded Cedar Crown’s European strike. Comments flooded the portal: Greek dockworkers offered anonymous testimony; Tarek’s cousins in Jordan spammed goat emojis; a Brussels analyst posted a legal brief in real time. Within minutes, Athens’ anti-proliferation unit arrived, tipped via Elena Vassos’ encrypted hotline. They seized the diamonds, the relics, and the shipping manifests linking the Cyclades clan to Maverick’s Aegean plan.

Greek Counterterror (EYP) agents in navy windbreakers swept the adjoining bay, cracking open crates labeled **MARBLE RESTORATION** to reveal cut-down AK receivers and Croatian-made pistol kits. A Coast Guard boarding team radioed from Piraeus that the partner freighter was already impounded. “Weapons smuggling charge sticks harder than art fraud,” Elena said dryly, snapping photos for Interpol. One EYP major whispered to Hunt, eyes wide, “This is the largest extremist financing seizure we’ve made since 2015.” Ioannis’ cell was no longer an open secret—it was a televised implosion of Maverick’s European war chest. The portal lit with headlines: **DIAMONDS, CASH, AND GUNS SEIZED IN ATHENS RAID**. Book Two’s stakes had just gone worldwide.

Ioannis tried to plead ignorance. Hunt leaned close, the Parthenon’s glow reflecting in the restorer’s terrified eyes. “Tell Maverick,” Hunt whispered, “that his diamond cache bought desalination pumps for Bedouins and fuel for Greek Coast Guard cutters. Every grudge you hold bankrolls us now.”

Outside, Leyla handed Barakat the satchel of seized cash. “Tarek wanted clean buses for his convoy,” she said. “Buy him a fleet.” Barakat saluted with two fingers. “Consider it done.”

Hunt looked toward Piraeus, where container cranes blinked like runway lights. Aegean Lock was already in motion, but tonight they’d slammed shut one hinge. Far off, ferries honked as they departed for the islands that would become their next battlefield. Hunt cracked his knuckles. “Back on course,” he said softly. “Next stop: the Cyclades.”

Elena caught up before they left. “The Hellenic Ministry of Culture saw your ‘restoration’ skills,” she said. “They have a cache of WWII weapons rotting in a government bunker. Help us stabilize them for museums and you’ll get official cover—and access to whatever contraband we find in the process.” Leyla arched an eyebrow. “State-sponsored weapons rehab? Finally, a respectable smuggling job.” Hunt nodded. “Tell them we’ll bring Saint Barbara.” Athens had gone from battleground to staging ground, and the Greeks were now co-conspirators.

### 31. Monastery Vault
The Hellenic Ministry’s “restoration project” lived inside an abandoned NATO ammunition bunker carved beneath a Meteora monastery. Hunt’s convoy zigzagged up switchbacks flanked by sheer cliffs and monk-tended gardens. A priest in a windbreaker met them at the gate, kissing Leyla’s Saint Barbara medallion before handing over a ring of rusted keys. “We kept the rifles oiled,” he said. “We could not keep the smugglers away.”

Inside, rows of WWII Enfields and MG‑34s lay on wooden racks. Half had been cannibalized—barrels missing, receivers replaced with 3D-printed blanks. Yara ran a Geiger wand over crates, frowning. “Someone has been swapping museum pieces with modern knockoffs,” she said. “Ioannis’ network extended up here.”

While Leyla and Elena catalogued the real relics, Hunt and Yael discovered a hidden false wall behind a row of Greek Civil War posters. They pried it open to reveal a cold-storage vault humming with modern electronics. Inside: cryo crates stamped **BLUE TRIREME SHIPPING**—the Cyclades clan’s logo. Each crate held modular drone warheads and smart detonators disguised as icon frames. Worse, a live data uplink pulsed from the vault to a yacht anchored off Mykonos.

Barakat whistled. “Twist of the day: the Greeks invited us to fix history, and we just found tomorrow’s weapons cache in their basement.”

Hunt texted Maya a photo of the crates. Her reply came fast: **BLUE TRIREME = Cyprus-based front. Tied to French banker we flagged last fall. Suggest seizing crates quietly, plant trackers, and let them deliver us to the Cyclades deal.**

They packed the modern crates into Ministry vans under the guise of “restoration shipping,” leaving trackers Leyla cooked up from Brother Elias’ Steyr bolts. As the vans rolled downhill, Hunt briefed the team. “We let Blue Trireme think their shipment survived. We ride it to the Mykonos handoff, then flip the entire network.”

Leyla grinned. “Restoring history by hijacking the future. I’m in.”

### 32. Mykonos Ghost Net
Blue Trireme’s handoff point lay inside a Mykonos superyacht marina where DJs thumped and influencers live-streamed obliviously. Hunt and Leyla disguised themselves as art restorers escorting the “icon crates” onto the yacht *Argonaut VIII*, a floating palace owned by shipping heir Nikos Karalis. Barakat posed as Greek Coast Guard liaison, clipboard in hand.

Once aboard, Hunt followed the tracker signal into the yacht’s spa deck, where Karalis toasted Maverick over holographic charts. The crates sat beside a hot tub, guarded by the Uzbek fixer’s cousin and two Russians in linen suits. Leyla set down a crate, “accidentally” knocking over a champagne bucket to short the yacht’s Wi-Fi. Yara slid into the network via the marina’s mesh, hijacking every camera for the Coalition portal.

Karalis smirked. “You’re the famous restorer,” he said to Hunt. “I thought you fixed churches, not geopolitics.”

“Turns out nukes and icons both need steady hands,” Hunt replied. He tapped his comm. “Barakat, net them.”

On cue, Greek Coast Guard boats cut their sirens and drifted in silent as sharks. Divers from a Hellenic anti-smuggling unit surfaced around the yacht, stringing a literal ghost net—monofilament webbing Leyla designed—around the hull to prevent escape. Barakat strode onto the gangway with Elena Vassos and EYP agents. “Mr. Karalis, by royal decree you are under arrest for trafficking weapons and conflict diamonds,” he announced.

The Russians reached for pistols; Hunt and Yael dropped them with non-lethal rounds. The Uzbek cousin lunged for the crates, only to find them empty save for Leyla’s trackers and a Saint Barbara icon. “Surprise,” she said, livestreaming his arrest to an audience that now included Greek parliamentarians cheering in the comments.

Karalis tried to bargain. “Blue Trireme funds entire islands,” he hissed. “Shut us down and you shut down tourism.”

“You fund terror cells,” Elena shot back. “Tourists can party without C-4 under their hotel.” She cited Greek statutes as EYP cuffed the smugglers. The Coalition portal feed exploded with headlines: **LUXURY YACHT RAID – BLUE TRIREME CEO DETAINED**.

Hunt watched the coast guard tow *Argonaut VIII* toward Piraeus. “Karalis was Maverick’s Cyclades wallet,” he said quietly. “Now it’s frozen.” Leyla clinked her water bottle against his. “On to the next smuggler,” she said. The ghost net tightened around Cedar Crown a little more.

### 33. Cyclades Caches
Karalis sang faster than anyone expected. By dawn he’d handed over GPS pins for Blue Trireme’s coastal caches—hidden in sea caves on Naxos, Paros, and Amorgos where smugglers stashed weapons between party seasons. Greek Coast Guard commodore Sofia Drakos met Hunt at a quay in Rafina, slapping a chart onto a plastic table. “Three caves. Three tides. We hit them before sunset or the yacht crews pull the crates,” she said.

Cache One sat inside a basalt blowhole on Naxos reachable only at low tide. Hunt rode with Greek divers on RHIBs while Leyla flew a microdrone through the narrow cave mouth, the drone’s camera catching phosphorescent algae glowing on the ceiling. Inside, surge pools slammed against the walls, half-flooding stainless drums packed with cobalt rods and tunnel maps for Mykonos hotels. EYP agents waded through ankle-deep seawater to seal the drums, and Leyla spray-painted the Cedar Coalition logo on the slick cave wall for the livestream audience.

Cache Two on Paros masqueraded as a wine cellar beneath a cliffside resort. Elena Vassos posed as a tax auditor, flanked by Yael and Barakat in resort uniforms. They discovered pallets of drone wings and suitcases stuffed with diamonds. Resort guests filmed the raid, turning it into instant PR. “Nothing ruins brunch like a weapons cache,” Leyla quipped on the portal feed.

Cache Three on Amorgos required Hunt and Noura to rappel into a sea cave where a Blue Trireme engineer had built a secret distillery to mask chemical smells. The cavern walls shimmered with salt crystals, and condensation dripped onto copper tubing hissing with fermented mash. Inside, they found printed guidance-tree schematics coated in seawater and a ledger showing payouts to the French banker Maya flagged. Noura held the schematics up to the livestream, explaining how Cedar Crown planned to hide drone detonators inside desalination plants across the Aegean. Comments flooded in from Greek mayors offering harbor access and volunteers.

By nightfall, Commodore Drakos announced that the Cyclades caches were secure and the seized goods—cobalt, diamonds, drone parts—were already cataloged for Interpol. “You shut down more than smugglers,” she told Hunt. “You shut down trust in the shipping clan.” Hunt nodded. “Trust doesn’t regenerate,” he said. “Fear does. Let’s keep feeding that.”

### 34. Santorini Relay
Blue Trireme’s financier—French private banker Lucien Vautrin—operated from a cliffside villa on Santorini, using a volcanic geothermal plant as his personal data relay. Maya’s intercepts showed him rerouting Cedar Crown funds through submarine cables anchored beneath the caldera. If they took Vautrin, the entire European funding tree collapsed.

Hunt, Leyla, Yael, and Elena flew in on a Greek Air Force tiltrotor at dusk, skimming the caldera rim before setting down on a helipad disguised as a heli-tour stop. Barakat and Commodore Drakos approached by sea in a coast guard cutter, their radios silent.

Leyla wore a museum conservator badge and carried a Saint Nicholas icon as cover. Hunt posed as her security detail. They walked straight into Vautrin’s gala, where influencers sipped volcanic wine while servers ferried trays past a glass wall showcasing the caldera. Beyond the glass, the geothermal plant’s exhaust plumes glowed red.

Vautrin, tuxedo sharp, kissed Leyla’s knuckles. “You bring me icons, I bring the world electricity,” he said. Hunt smiled politely while Yael slipped a fiber tap into Vautrin’s armored server rack.

Maya fed instructions into Hunt’s earpiece. “Patch me into the geothermal relay. I’ll reroute his cable traffic to our portal while the Greek Revenue Service hits his accounts.” Hunt clipped the tap to the rack, and Leyla triggered a distraction—she “accidentally” spilled volcanic ash onto a guest’s designer shoes, sending half the gala into chaos. In the confusion, Elena served Vautrin with a Greek warrant citing weapons financing and environmental fraud.

Vautrin tried to run, ducking into the geothermal plant’s maintenance tunnel. Hunt and Barakat chased him through sulfur-scented corridors lit by red emergency lights. Yael cut power above, forcing Vautrin to stumble in darkness. Outside, Commodore Drakos’ cutter lit the caldera with searchlights, blocking any escape by sea.

On a catwalk above molten vents, Vautrin spun, pistol shaking. “Kill me and you lose every password,” he hissed.

Hunt raised his hands. “Or you hand them over and we let French regulators treat you kindly.” Behind Vautrin, Leyla rappelled from the exhaust tower, kicked him square in the back, and disarmed him. Noura, patched in from Muscat, guided Hunt through the relay’s kill-switch. Within minutes, Cedar Crown’s European accounts froze, and the Cedar Coalition portal flashed a new headline: **SANTORINI RAID CUTS OFF EUROPEAN TERROR FUNDING**.

Vautrin stared at the caldera, realizing half his guests were undercover cops and the rest were livestreaming his downfall. Elena read him his rights in French. Hunt looked toward the dark sea. “Phase Aegean just lost its banker,” he said. “Now we go for the pilots.”

### 35. Amphibious Vector
With the banker in custody, Maya pushed the next lead: a fleet of refurbished Beriev Be‑12 amphibious planes hidden on a decommissioned Hellenic Navy runway on the island of Skyros. Cedar Crown planned to use the planes’ long range to hop reactors between Mediterranean coves. Hunt’s job was to catch the pilots before they launched.

Leyla and Barakat infiltrated Skyros under the cover of a Coast Guard “firefighting drill,” bringing along Greek Air Force Major Eleni Stavros and two Hellenic K9 teams—Athena the Belgian Malinois and Thanos the German Shepherd. The dogs wore Coast Guard harnesses rigged with radiation sensors and C-4 sniffers. They arrived at dawn to find the amphibious planes painted in the livery of a humanitarian aid NGO. Mechanics loaded crates labeled **EMERGENCY DESALINATION UNITS**—the same crates Ashur had used in Morocco. Athena immediately pawed at a crate, revealing a hidden charge wired to Maverick’s dead-man switch; Leyla diffused it before the pilots taxied.

Hunt snuck beneath one fuselage, planting sugar-gel cartridges in the hydraulic lines. Yael climbed into the cockpit, downloading the flight plan through a diagnostic port. “They’re hopping Skyros → Crete → Benghazi → Marseille,” she whispered. “Four reactors, four capitals.”

Just as they prepared to seize the planes, Maverick appeared on the tarmac wearing a Greek flight suit, flanked by two devgru washouts. Athena bared her teeth; Thanos strained at his lead. “I figured you’d follow the money,” Maverick called out. “Let’s see if you can swim.”

He fired a flare gun, signaling the pilots to take off early. Engines roared. Hunt sprinted up the loading ramp of the nearest Beriev, tackled the pilot, and wrestled for the controls while the plane jerked forward onto the sea. Leyla triggered the sugar-gel; hydraulic fluid foamed, forcing the plane to settle back into the water. On shore, Greek Air Force commandos stormed the runway, trading shots with Maverick’s escort.

Maverick dove into another amphibious plane and tried to take off. Barakat commandeered a firefighting helicopter, hovering above the plane and dropping retardant directly onto its engines. The amphibian sputtered, skidded across the water, and slammed into a pier, leaving Maverick scrambling into the surf. Thanos dragged his handler toward a fuel cache hidden under camouflage netting—inside sat hydrazine canisters and satphones. Leyla tagged them for detonation while Athena tracked Maverick’s blood trail to the RHIB he used to escape, ensuring the Coast Guard had a heading.

Yael aimed a flare pistol at the fuel farm, warning Maverick. “Surrender or we torch your fleet.” Maverick grinned and dove beneath the waves toward a waiting RHIB, vanishing yet again—but without his planes.

Greek authorities seized three intact amphibious aircraft and the manifests showing future reactor routes. Hunt looked at Leyla. “No planes, no hopscotch,” he said. Leyla nodded. “Maverick keeps swimming. We keep draining the pool.”

### 36. Pilot Snare
Maya’s analysis of the seized manifests pointed to a finishing school for mercenary pilots tucked inside an old NATO radar site on Crete’s southern ridge. Cedar Crown rotated their best aviators through the school every ten days, teaching them how to fly low over EU borders and parachute reactors into hidden coves. With the amphibious fleet grounded, Maverick would race there next. Hunt decided to get there first.

Greek Special Forces Major Eleni Stavros arranged a joint raid with Cedar Coalition, Mossad, and Jordanian SOF. They dirt-biked through olive groves under cover of a sirocco wind. Athena and Thanos rode in armored canine carriers strapped to Leyla’s Hilux, ears perked for command.

The radar site—call sign TRIDENT ECHO in old NATO maps—still bristled with satellite dishes. Maverick’s pilots lounged in the shadow of the main antenna, comparing GPS spoofing scripts. A French expat named Colette ran the classroom, projecting approach plates for Marseille, Palermo, and Algiers. Hunt listened from a ventilation shaft while Yael patched into the projector feed, swapping the maps for a live Cedar Coalition stream of their own faces. When the pilots looked up, they saw Hunt, Leyla, and Eleni staring back at them.

“Class dismissed,” Leyla said over the speakers, then blew the door hinges with a micro-charge. Athena bounded in, tackling a pilot who reached for a Glock. Thanos cornered Colette against a rack of radar gear. Greek commandos fast-roped through the skylight, flashbangs popping.

One pilot bolted out the back and jumped on an ATV. Barakat fired up his own bike and gave chase across the scrub, narrating the pursuit to the portal feed. “Pilot heading for the canyon,” he barked. Yael took to the air in a loitering drone, feeding a live bird’s-eye view to the Coalition audience. Viewers spammed FAFO memes as Barakat nudged the ATV into a ravine where EYP agents waited with cuffs.

Inside the classroom, Colette threw a satchel of euros at Hunt. “Take it,” she begged. “I only flew for money.”

“Money’s worthless when the world knows your face,” Hunt replied, cuffing her wrists. He motioned to Eleni. “Your airfield.”

Eleni stepped up to the podium, switching the projector to a live feed of the Greek Parliament praising the raid. “By authority of the Hellenic Republic, you are under arrest for conspiracy to commit nuclear blackmail,” she declared. The pilots’ shoulders sagged. Tarek’s Bedouin channel flooded the portal chat with goat emojis again, celebrating the capture.

As prisoners were escorted out, Maya broke in over comms. “One pilot is missing: Maverick’s prodigy, codename Alcyon. Drone feed shows a single wingsuit glider launching from the back ridge, heading toward the sea.”

Hunt sprinted outside, spotting the dark silhouette gliding toward the Aegean. Leyla passed him a flare gun. “Can’t shoot a parachute,” she warned.

“No,” Hunt said, pulling out a microdrone launcher instead. He fired; the drone unspooled a monofilament tether that tangled in Alcyon’s wingsuit. The glider spiraled, splashing into a shallow bay where Greek rescue swimmers grabbed the pilot before he could deploy a cyanide tooth. Thanos barked approval from the ridge.

With the pilot cadre arrested and their school exposed, Hunt took a moment to breathe. “Pilots down,” he transmitted to Maya. “Next, we hunt the engineers.”

### 37. Hellenic Arsenal
Before chasing the engineers, Hunt honored the promise Elena brokered: help the Greek government restore and catalog its WWII weapons cache. The Ministry of Culture opened the Hellenic Arsenal, a sprawling underground complex near Thessaloniki where rusting Sten guns, Panzerfausts, and British PIAT launchers lay under tarps. Officially, the Cedar Coalition team was there to stabilize artifacts. Unofficially, the arsenal doubled as a neutral workshop where Greek technicians, Bedouin mechanics, and Mossad armorers could swap knowledge—and sniff out any Cedar Crown sleeper agents embedded in the bureaucracy.

Greek conservator Dr. Katerina Vlachos greeted them wearing nitrile gloves and a welding mask. “You saved our islands,” she said. “Now help us save our history.” She led Hunt and Leyla to a row of disassembled MG‑34s destined for a museum in Larissa. The guns’ receivers were pitted with rust; their springs were missing. Hunt opened his Saint Barbara box, pulling out Steyr bolts and MG-42 recoil springs they’d pocketed in Beirut. “Courtesy of Brother Elias,” he said, handing them over. Katerina’s eyes widened. “Holy relics?”

“Holy enough to fool customs,” Leyla replied. They spent the afternoon degreasing parts, fabricating new springs on Noura’s portable lathe, and teaching Greek apprentices how to reverse-engineer suppressor baffles hidden inside carved icons. Athena napped beside a crate of PIAT rockets while Thanos guarded the entrance with a laser-pointer toy. When a training alert blared—rumors of a Cedar Crown cell casing the arsenal’s loading dock—Hunt grabbed one of the freshly restored MG‑34s, test-fired a burst into a ballistic sponge, and smiled. “Still sings,” he said. Thankfully the alert proved false, but for twenty tense minutes the Coalition stood ready to defend the arsenal with museum pieces. The anecdote later lit up the portal feed as “#StBarbaraStillWorks.”

During the restoration, Hunt noticed a nervous Ministry clerk hovering near a stack of shipping manifests. He recognized the name embossed on the clerk’s lanyard: an engineer who’d once interviewed for Cedar Crown’s reactor program. Hunt quietly signaled Barakat, who drifted over and engaged the clerk in small talk. Within minutes, Barakat had the clerk on a secure line with Greek counterintelligence, confirming that Cedar Crown had tried—and failed—to recruit him. “They promised me a grant,” the clerk admitted. “I told them I’d rather paint icons.”

The arsenal visit wasn’t just goodwill; it was counter-messaging. Leyla streamed a short video of Greek conservators restoring MG‑34s with Coalition parts, tagging it #RestoringHistory. Comments poured in from museum-goers and militia veterans alike. Maya whispered in Hunt’s ear, “Public sentiment shifting. Even Ankara-friendly blogs are praising the Cooperation.”

As they wrapped up, Katerina handed Hunt a newly restored Sten Mk II. “Keep this in your Greek cover story,” she said. “It survived two wars and a dictatorship. It deserves to travel with you.” Hunt accepted the gift, sliding it into a custom case. “We’ll bring it back when Cedar Crown dies,” he promised.

With public goodwill secured and the arsenal buzzing with allied cooperation, Hunt felt the coalition’s roots sinking deeper into Greek soil. Next on his list: the engineers designing Maverick’s next-generation reactors.

### 38. Anemoi Architects
Noura’s data pulled the thread. Maverick’s “engineers” weren’t hiding in labs—they embedded themselves inside a cleantech consortium called Anemoi Architects, headquartered on the wind-scoured island of Tinos. Publicly, Anemoi designed microgrids and desalination turbines. Privately, they prototyped Cedar Crown’s next modular reactor core, code-named Marine Atlas. Their lead designer: Dr. Calliope Arvanitis, a celebrity Greek engineer who once sat on EU energy panels and publicly sparred with Noura. Behind Calliope was Maverick’s shadow pilot, Alcyon—the wingsuit prodigy now in custody but refusing to talk.

Hunt decided to leverage both. They flew Alcyon to Tinos in shackles, staging a “prisoner tour” to draw Calliope out. Meanwhile, Yara and Maya spoofed an EU energy grant email inviting Anemoi to present their Marine Atlas prototype at a “secret” session.

The Anemoi campus clung to Tinos’ cliffs, its glass walls reflecting the Aegean. Hunt, Leyla, Noura, and Barakat walked in with Ministry escorts. Athena and Thanos remained outside with Greek police, muzzles off, ready. Alcyon, cuffed and flanked by Mossad guards, looked pale but defiant.

Calliope met them in the atrium, flanked by engineers wearing linen suits and anti-static sneakers. “I see you brought my former student,” she said, nodding at Alcyon. “He lacks vision.”

“He lacks freedom,” Leyla shot back. “We’re here for your prototypes.”

Calliope led them through a gallery of benign projects—wind turbines, desal systems—then into Marine Atlas lab: a cavern lined with blue LED strips, the centerpiece a half-scale modular reactor core suspended over a saltwater pool. Noura’s breath caught. “You stole my open-source design and weaponized it,” she said softly.

Calliope shrugged. “I monetized it. Qatar wanted one for a floating city. Cedar Crown paid more.” She glanced at Alcyon. “Don’t you wish you’d stayed loyal?”

Alcyon looked down. He’d refused to speak during debriefs, but seeing Calliope ignited something. “You told me reactors would end wars,” he whispered. “Instead you strapped them to Maverick’s ego.”

Hunt seized the moment. “Help us take her down,” he murmured. Alcyon hesitated, then nodded. “Marine Atlas has a failsafe. Short the coolant sensors and the core shuts down,” he said quietly, reciting a code phrase he and Calliope used. Noura memorized it instantly.

Leyla signaled Yara. The hacker injected a worm into Anemoi’s network via a fake firmware update. Screens flickered. Calliope frowned—then realized too late that her entire facility was broadcasting live to the Cedar Coalition portal. EU energy regulators chimed in with shocked emojis.

Calliope backed toward the pool. “You can’t arrest me. I sit on three EU boards,” she warned.

Elena Vassos stepped into frame, brandishing a dual warrant signed by Athens and Brussels. “You sat,” Elena said. “Now you kneel.”

Engineers scattered. Barakat and Yael moved to detain them, while Hunt and Noura circled the Marine Atlas core. Noura keyed Alcyon’s failsafe phrase into the console. The core’s LEDs dimmed, coolant valves locking. “Your toy is safe,” she said to Calliope. “But it’s ours now.”

Calliope lunged for a hidden pistol under the console. Athena slammed into her before she could fire, TEETH clamping onto her forearm. Thanos pinned another engineer trying to delete hard drives. The dogs had become full-fledged Coalition members, their live camera feeds showing the takedown from ground level.

Hunt leaned in, voice low. “Marine Atlas is done. Your EU friends just watched you weaponize humanitarian tech. What’s left?”

Calliope spat blood. “Maverick still has engineers in Marseille,” she hissed. “You can’t be everywhere.”

“Watch us,” Leyla replied, cuffing her wrists.

After the raid, Hunt visited Alcyon’s cell. “Appreciate the failsafe,” he said. Alcyon stared at the floor. “Let my family know I tried to do one thing right,” he whispered. Hunt nodded. “Consider it done.” On his way out, he slung the restored Sten Mk II Katerina loaned him—the same weapon he’d dry-fired in the arsenal—and promised himself he’d return it to the museum once Cedar Crown fell.

Maya patched in with the next lead: “Calliope ratted out a Marseille shipyard where Cedar Crown is building floating reactor pods. Engage when ready.” Hunt looked at Noura and Leyla. “Pilots down. Engineers captured. Next: the shipwrights.”

### 39. Thames Detour
Marseille never happened—at least not yet. As Hunt’s team prepped to raid the French shipyard, Athena’s handler flagged an encrypted call routed through London’s Docklands. Cedar Crown wasn’t moving reactor pods from Marseille; they were buying rare parts from a clandestine London auction masquerading as a Victorian salvage sale. Worse, MI5 intercepted chatter that Maverick planned to grab hostages—a group of EU energy ministers touring HMS Belfast—to force a “ceasefire” in Cedar Crown raids.

The coalition pivoted overnight. London’s skyline greeted Hunt with drizzle, sirens, and a taste of old ghosts. Leyla slipped into a Restoration Auction under the guise of representing a Greek museum. Barakat and Yael shadowed her, while Athena and Thanos flew in with UK police K9 handlers. Eleni Stavros coordinated with MI5’s Thames Counterterror team.

The auction hall—an old warehouses near Tower Bridge—displayed Victorian diving helmets, Enigma machines, and disassembled submarine parts. In a back room, Maverick’s broker sold heat-resistant alloys perfect for Marine Atlas casings. Hunt spotted a familiar face among the bidders: Prince Talal’s former banker, freshly disgraced. Maverick was recycling every contact.

Leyla signaled with a tap on her wine glass. “Hostage package en route,” she whispered into Hunt’s comm. Through a half-open loading bay, he saw Cedar Crown mercs disguised as Thames tour guides steering a boat toward HMS Belfast, its deck currently packed with EU ministers in bulletproof coats. Maverick planned to stage a “peace talk” gone wrong.

Hunt split the team. Yael, Barakat, Athena, and Thanos boarded a River Police RIB to intercept the tour boat. Leyla and Noura stayed inside the auction to seize the reactor parts. Hunt paired with MI5 officer Saira Malik, racing along the riverwalk with MPXs concealed under raincoats.

At the auction, Noura triggered the fire alarm, sending wealthy buyers scrambling. Leyla grabbed the alloy ingots and stuffed them into museum shipping crates, all while livestreaming the auctioneer’s stunned face. “London, meet Cedar Crown’s shopping spree,” she said on the portal feed. Comments exploded from British viewers demanding answers.

On the Thames, Maverick’s mercs drew suppressed MP7s and forced the tour boat alongside HMS Belfast. Athena leapt from the police RIB, clamping onto the leg of one gunman before he could board. Thanos bounded across the deck, knocking another merc—and his detonator—into the river. Barakat and Yael fast-roped from a police chopper, securing the ministers while MI5 snipers on City Hall’s roof used MOD-issued Barretts loaded with EXACTO rounds to steer bullets around antenna masts and into the mercs’ plate gaps despite the crosswind whipping off the river.citeturn2search0turn2search3

Maverick himself wasn’t on the boat. Instead, a pre-recorded holo flickered from a tablet: “Call off your raids or London burns,” his image warned, before the tablet dissolved in seawater. Hunt shook his head. “Classic,” he muttered.

MI5 thanked the Coalition publicly, staging a press conference right on the HMS Belfast deck. Prime Minister Hale (from Book One’s White House subplot) called in live, promising expanded intel sharing. Leyla wheeled the seized alloy crates into view, explaining how London’s own black market nearly armed Maverick. The portal feed peaked at 200k viewers.

That night, Hunt stood on Tower Bridge, rain soaking his coat, Saint Barbara icon cold against his chest. Tarek texted a selfie of Bedouins watching the London stream on their phones. “We’re global now,” Leyla said quietly beside him. “Pilots, engineers, shipwrights, bankers—Maverick’s running out of tiers.”

“He still has one,” Hunt replied. “Hostages.” He watched the Thames flow past, lit by the Eye and City Hall. “Next time he won’t use proxies.”

### 40. Fusion Hostage
“Next time” arrived twelve hours later. Maya’s feed lit up with an SOS tagged **IRN-FUSION**. A rogue wing of Iran’s IRGC Quds Force had been quietly buying fusion-grade tritium capsules through Cedar Crown’s auction chain. When MI6 shuttered the Docklands sale, the wing panicked and grabbed five scientists from the Culham Centre for Fusion Energy outside Oxford—Britain’s leading fusion lab—to force a trade: release their tritium shipment and let Maverick’s partners keep operating, or the scientists die in a mock fusion detonation staged for propaganda.

Complicating the hostage crisis, Iran’s official government denied involvement and offered back-channel cooperation to avoid blame. Hunt had to neutralize the rogue unit without igniting a diplomatic incident.

Leyla, Noura, Barakat, Athena, and Thanos staged out of RAF Benson with SAS hostage rescuers. Yael coordinated with Mossad’s London station, while MI5 cordoned Culham. Iranian reformist liaisons met Hunt in a secure hangar, hands raised, offering intelligence on the rogue unit’s commander: General Reza Darvishi, disgraced after opposing the Cedar Coalition raids. He intended to use the kidnapped scientists to assemble a black-market fusion package for Maverick.

Hunt, Leyla, and the SAS team slipped through Culham’s service tunnels while MI5 negotiators stalled Darvishi with a fake tritium manifest. Noura accessed the fusion lab’s control system, ready to shut down any improvised plasma reaction.

In the main tokamak hall, Darvishi broadcast via encrypted livestream, forcing one scientist to read a manifesto blaming Western “pirates.” Athena crouched beside Hunt, ready to sprint. Hunt watched through thermal scope: six hostiles, five hostages, a mock plasma injector wired with conventional explosives meant to mimic a fusion flash.

Leyla whispered, “We’re not letting this go live.” Hunt nodded. He signaled the SAS to stack on the door, while Barakat guided Thanos toward a side entrance to sniff for explosives. Thanos detected C-4 strapped to the catwalk. Noura relayed the circuit layout, describing how Darvishi planned to fake a fusion burst—enough to terrify governments into backing off.

On Hunt’s mark, the SAS tossed flashbangs while Athena dashed toward the nearest hostage, clamping onto a gunman’s arm. Thanos leapt onto the catwalk, ripping out det cords. Hunt ripped Darvishi’s rifle free and fired point-blank into the rogue general’s chest before he could hit a dead-man switch. Leyla used a sugar-gel syringe to jam the mock plasma injector, rendering it inert while SAS operators double-tapped the remaining IRGC militants.

Darvishi gasped once, eyes wide, then went still. Hunt kicked the dead man’s detonator into the containment pit. “Consider your rogue wing disbanded,” he muttered.

MI5 whisked the scientists to safety while Leyla streamed a short clip of Athena and Thanos licking the scientists’ hands. Comments flooded the portal, praising the “fusion dogs.”

Prime Minister Hale called again, this time flanked by Iran’s reformist ambassador on a joint statement broadcast. “This was an attack on science,” Hale said. “Together with the Cedar Coalition and responsible Iranian officials, we stopped it.” It was the first time a Western leader publicly credited Iran in the coalition fight—a twist Maverick hadn’t anticipated.

After the rescue, Noura stood inside the tokamak’s blue glow, eyes reflecting the containment field. “We saved fusion,” she whispered. Hunt nodded. “And we made Maverick’s circle even smaller.”

Maya pinged with an epilogue: “Darvishi’s laptop shows flights to Marseille are canceled. Maverick’s builders are scrambling. Ready for the shipyard?” Hunt looked at Leyla, Athena, and Thanos. “After this, yeah,” he said. “Let’s end it.”

### 41. Marseille Council
“Ending it” required a council of every ally they’d collected. Hunt convened the meeting inside a repurposed hangar at RAF Northolt before wheels-up to France. Around the table sat Leyla, Noura, Barakat, Yael, Maya’s holo avatar, MI5 officer Saira Malik, Greek Major Eleni Stavros, Commodore Drakos (via secure video), and Athena and Thanos curled at Hunt’s feet. They all looked exhausted; none looked ready to quit.

Hunt opened the session. “We have one shot at the Marseille shipyard,” he said, voice low but steady. “Calliope’s files show the pods are ninety percent complete. Maverick lost his pilots and bankroll, so he’ll be desperate. Leyla, what’s the shipping picture?”

Leyla slid a tablet onto the table, displaying a 3D model of the Port of Marseille. “Blue Trireme rerouted three barges under EU food-aid exemptions,” she said. “Each barge hides a reactor pod. They’ll load them onto a Liberian-flagged freighter tomorrow at 0400. We intercept tonight, before they leave the dry dock. I’ve planted trackers on the crane controllers, but we need eyes on the water.”

Commodore Drakos’ video feed crackled. “My patrol boats can sit just outside French territorial waters,” she offered. “If the freighter bolts, we chase it into open sea. But I need French cover.”

Saira Malik nodded. “GCHQ already tipped DGSI,” she said. “France knows we’re coming, but they want deniability. Translation: if we mess up, it’s our mess.”

Maya’s holo winked. “Good thing we don’t mess up,” she said. “Noura, walk them through Marine Atlas vulnerabilities.”

Noura tapped the table, bringing up schematics. “The pods use the same coolant pathway as Calliope’s prototype,” she explained. “If we can inject sugar gel into the intake before they leave the dock, the pods shut down. But we have to do it quietly—the pods are rigged with anti-tamper charges. My plan: Hunt and I swim under the hull with gel cartridges, Leyla and Yael hack the crane PLCs, Barakat leads the assault team topside.”

Barakat raised an eyebrow. “Swimming?” he asked. “Last time I swam was in Aqaba, and that ended with us stealing diamonds.”

“Consider it a sequel,” Leyla said. “You get to wear a wetsuit this time.”

Eleni leaned in. “What about Maverick?” she asked. “He vanished at Skyros. Intelligence suggests he’s already in Marseille, disguised as a French harbor pilot. If he boards that freighter, he disappears into the Atlantic.”

Yael responded before Hunt could. “Then we yank every pilot card off the table,” she said. “Maverick likes posing as a savior. If we hijack the harbor control channel and announce an emergency evacuation, he’ll have to reveal himself to keep the ship moving.”

Hunt considered it. “Works,” he said. “Athena and Thanos handle security on the docks. Noura and I handle the pods. Leyla and Yael run the cranes. Barakat, you command the assault stack with SAS and DGSI. Saira, you keep London and Paris synced. Maya, you jam Blue Trireme’s comms.”

Leyla leaned back. “Before we move, say it plainly,” she said. “No hostage deals, no mercy. Anyone tied to Cedar Crown on that dock doesn’t leave breathing.”

Hunt met her eyes. “Agreed,” he said. “We’ve killed every cell we’ve touched. Marseille won’t be different.”

Noura looked around the table. “Then let me say what we’re fighting for,” she said softly. “Not just reactors and dogs and saints. We’re fighting so the next engineer doesn’t have to wonder if her work will be weaponized. That’s why I keep showing up. That’s why I keep trusting you.”

Barakat grinned. “And I’m here because I owe you both too many drinks to walk away,” he said. Laughter rippled—short, tired, but real.

Maya’s holo flickered. “Thirty minutes until wheels-up,” she announced. “Once you leave, comms go dark until you hit the dock. Last words?”

Athena barked once. Thanos followed with a howl. Leyla scratched their ears. “We’ll take that as ‘bring back treats,’” she said.

Hunt looked at the team. “Marseille, then home,” he said. “Saint Barbara willing.” They rose in unison, each scooping up gear: Leyla’s drone case, Noura’s waterproof satchel, Barakat’s MPX, Yael’s lock picks, Maya’s encrypted tablets, Saira’s MI5 credentials, Eleni’s Greek flag patch. Athena and Thanos trotted beside them, tails high.

As they filed toward the hangar doors, Leyla whispered to Hunt, “Whatever happens, you’re buying the first round in Mykonos.”

“If Mykonos still has bars by the time we’re done,” Hunt replied. For the first time in days, he smiled. Then the hangar doors opened, the night air hit their faces, and silence fell. Marseille awaited, and with it the chance to end Cedar Crown’s reign—or die trying.

### 42. Dry Dock Ghosts
Marseille’s Grand Port glowed like a neon maze when the coalition slipped in under a moonless sky. Noura and Hunt coasted toward Dry Dock 8 on diver scooters, sugar-gel cartridges strapped to their thighs. Above them, Leyla and Yael perched atop a ship-to-shore crane control room, laptops out, drone feed overlaying the dock with call signs. Barakat led the SAS/DGSI stack toward the gate. Athena and Thanos padded beside them, ears alert.

Unexpected twist #1 came before the first shot: as Hunt and Noura approached the hull, they saw two kids—teenage stowaways—sneaking out from under the freighter. “We hid in the bilge,” the girl whispered when she spotted Hunt’s rebreather. “They said they’d take us to London.” Noura cursed softly. “Get them to Barakat,” Hunt said. Leyla, listening in, redirected Athena to escort the kids to safety. “Stay with the dog,” she told them over comms. “If you run, she herds you by biting.” The kids obeyed.

Sugar gel injected, Hunt and Noura climbed the dry dock ladder, surfacing behind a stack of reactor pod shells. Barakat’s voice crackled. “Gate breached. No alarms yet.” Leyla hacked the crane PLCs, freezing their movement. Yael injected a faint signal into the harbor pilot channel. “Attention: chemical spill at Dock 6,” she announced in perfect Marseille French. “Evacuate immediately.” Dock workers scattered like ants, leaving Cedar Crown’s security detail exposed.

Maverick, disguised as a harbor pilot, realized the ruse. He strode onto the dock, ripping off his high-vis vest. “You really brought everyone,” he shouted, spotting Hunt and Leyla. “What’s next, the Pope?”

“Just Saint Barbara,” Leyla replied, snapping the cedar icon to the front of her plate carrier. “You’re fresh out of miracles.”

Unexpected twist #2 erupted from the freighter’s superstructure: an entire squad of VolgaShield mercs armed with suppressed UMPs—Maverick’s “insurance.” They’d bribed a DGSI liaison to clear their arrival. Shots cracked. Barakat shouted, “Contact, starboard catwalk!” SAS shooters engaged, while Athena and Thanos charged the catwalk, knocking two mercs into the water. Hunt flanked along the hull, MPX barking. Noura tossed a thermite grenade into the open reactor pod, ensuring it would never work.

Maverick tried to flee up the crane ladder. Leyla jammed the crane controls, freezing him halfway. “Stuck?” she smirked.

He looked down. “You can’t kill me,” he taunted. “If I die, fail-safes in Ghana, Sicily, and Muscat trigger. I’ll torch half the ports you ‘saved.’”

Hunt climbed the ladder, breathing hard. “You had your chance to stop,” he said. “You chose this.” He rammed his shoulder into Maverick’s chest. They grappled atop the crane trolley, Marseille’s neon splashing below. Maverick reached for a hidden knife; Hunt slammed his hand against the steel, bones crunching. Leyla’s voice came through Hunt’s earpiece. “Barakat confirms all pods destroyed. Your call.”

Hunt thought of Culham’s scientists, of Noura’s students, of the Bedouin truckers, of Alcyon. “No hostages,” he said. “No mercy.” He drove Maverick off the crane. The former Navy officer plummeted, hit the deck with a sickening crunch, and lay still. Cedar Crown’s architect of chaos was dead.

Unexpected twist #3 surfaced seconds later: the DGSI liaison who had sold out to Maverick tried to detonate a backup charge on the freighter, panicking when the pods failed. Yael spotted him sprinting toward the blast mat. “Traitor!” she shouted. Athena barreled into him, teeth sinking into his wrist. Barakat finished the job with a burst. “No more surprises,” he growled.

Silence rolled over the dock as French police sirens wailed in the distance. Hunt climbed down, wiped blood off his knuckles, and looked at the destroyed pods. “Saint Barbara, you watching?” he muttered. “We did what we had to.”

Leyla joined him, sliding the icon back into his palm. “Miracles done,” she said. “Now we mop.”

Noura emerged from behind the pods, face streaked with salt. “Marine Atlas disabled,” she reported. “France’s energy minister already thanked us via encrypted text.” She looked at Maverick’s body. “He thought he was an engineer. He was just a bully with a bigger wrench.”

Maya came through on comms, voice vibrating with relief. “French media wants to credit DGSI,” she said. “Fine. Our portal has the real story. Cameras caught the kids hugging Athena. It’s trending.”

Barakat gathered the team. “Casualties?” he asked. “None on our side,” Leyla answered. “All Cedar Crown operatives neutralized.”

Saira Malik arrived with DGSI officers, nodding once at the carnage. “We’ll clean up,” she said. “You disappear.” She glanced at Maverick’s body. “France never met him. Officially.”

Hunt looked around the dock one last time: pods ruined, mercs dead, kids safe, dogs panting. “We’re done here,” he said. “Pack up.”

As they slipped back toward the rendezvous point, Leyla nudged Hunt. “About that round in Mykonos,” she said. “You owe me two now.”

Hunt exhaled, finally letting the weight settle. “I’ll buy the bar,” he said. They vanished into the Marseille night, Cedar Crown dismantled at last. Saint Barbara could rest—for now.

### 43. Black Tide Interlude
Victory barely cracked the headlines before the real-world fallout hit. Cedar Crown’s collapse triggered oil supply chaos: Blue Trireme’s shell companies had been controlling bunker fuel routes, and their sudden disappearance left Mediterranean ports scrambling. Tankers queued from Algeciras to Alexandria. Pirate crews—spun-up by Maverick months earlier—saw opportunity and began hijacking under-crewed product tankers off Tunisia.

Maya briefed the coalition from a Eurostar carriage back to London. “We cut off their reactors,” she said over a secure tablet, “but Maverick’s ‘insurance’ was commodity leverage. Three hijackings in 48 hours. If we don’t plug the gap, France, Italy, and North Africa start rationing fuel. Cedar Crown’s ghost still chokes supply.”

Hunt rubbed his temples. “We’re not tanker cops,” he said.

“No,” Leyla replied. “But those pirates were Cedar Crown-trained. Clean up our own mess.”

Commodore Drakos patched in. “Greek tanker *Eleni Stavros*—yes, named after our favorite major—was seized near Sfax. Twenty crew taken, cargo holds rigged with mines. Pirates demand safe passage and payment in crypto wallets tied to Blue Trireme. Iran denies involvement, but the hijackers’ comms match the rogue wing we just killed.”

Barakat leaned forward. “Then we finish them at sea. We still owe Tarek fuel.”

Hunt nodded slowly. “One more run,” he said. “We board the *Eleni*, rescue the crew, blow the mines, and prove Cedar Crown can’t choke the oil. Then we can actually rest.”

Leyla sighed. “Saint Barbara’s going to need a new icon,” she muttered, already planning the boarding.

### 44. Operation Black Tide
Two days later the coalition boarded a French Navy Atlantique patrol plane over the Strait of Sicily. Below, the Greek tanker *Eleni Stavros* drifted under the guns of half a dozen pirate skiffs lashed together with tarps. The pirates flew no flag, but their radios spouted out-of-band Farsi slang the coalition had heard in Culham—Darvishi’s surviving runners, now freelancing as oil hijackers. France, still fresh off seizing the Russian “shadow fleet” tanker *Grinch* the month before, wanted them neutralized quietly to show sanctions enforcement had teeth without sparking another diplomatic storm.citeturn0search1turn0search4

“Remind me why Greek tankers keep getting named after Eleni,” Barakat joked as the Atlantique’s ramp opened.

“Because Eleni keeps lending them to us,” Leyla replied, checking her rebreather. “Less talk, more splash.”
Hunt, Leyla, Yael, a four-man DEVGRU element on loan from SOCOM, and two French Commandos Marine fast-roped from the Atlantique onto the tanker’s aft deck while Athena and Thanos were winched down in harnesses, paws spread like parachutes. Simultaneously, a SEAL Mark V fast boat knifed through the swells, its twin diesels roaring as it flanked the pirate skiffs. “Vandal, boarding via RHIB,” the boat commander radioed. “Mark V suppressing targets at bearing 270.” Overhead, Seawolf flight—a mixed pair of MH-60S Armed Helo Seahawks from the destroyer’s HSC det—hammered low over the freeboard, AN/AAS-44 FLIR turrets painting every pickup while the air mission commander pushed Link 16 bursts to the escorting frigate. “Seawolf-One on station, Romeo pattern established,” the pilot called. Crew chiefs Kramer and Soto leaned into GAU-21 .50s on soft mounts as they shredded a skiff screening the mothership.citeturn3search4turn3search1 Forty meters higher, an MH-60R from HSM-74 held nose-on with AN/APS-153 radar sweeping for fresh fast-movers while the sensor operator kept the ALFS pod staged in case the pirates dumped contraband overboard; the tac-co piped every surface track straight into Hunt’s tablet so he could watch the battlespace redraw in real time.citeturn3search1turn3search3turn3search2 When a mothership tried to bolt for deeper water, Seawolf-Two’s weapons officer barked, “Rifle!” and kicked a Hellfire from the pylon, the missile spearing the dhow’s engine room and flipping it in a sheet of spray.citeturn3search4 The SEALs—call signs Bishop, Vandal, Latch, and Cruz—split into two assault pairs, one clearing the aft crane while the other secured the helo pad. “Stern secure,” Bishop whispered. Two French snipers lay prone in the Atlantique’s open ramp with loaner Barrett M107s fed by DARPA’s EXACTO .50-cal tracking-bullet prototypes, their optical seekers riding a laser spot while steerable fins corrected the round’s flight path so they could tag pirates across the heaving deck.citeturn2search0turn2search3 “Deck clear; pirate by manifold is mine,” one murmured before releasing a guided tracer that visibly arced around a mast guy-wire as the round’s actuator made course tweaks dozens of times per second, then punched through the gunman leaning over the railing.citeturn2search3 Noura and Barakat followed in a RHIB crewed by Italian Guardia Costiera, part of the EU’s ad hoc Aspides escort that had already rescued crews when Houthis hit the *Sounion* in the Red Sea the previous year.citeturn0search29

On deck, a pirate pointed his AK at the boarding team. Hunt didn’t hesitate—three rounds, target down. “No negotiations,” he said, voice flat. The remaining pirates scattered. Thanos lunged, dragging one down by the calf while Athena herded two others against the superstructure. Leyla slapped flex cuffs on their wrists.

Down in the pump room, Noura found the real problem: mines wired to the bunker fuel manifolds. “They planned to cripple supply,” she said. “Bunker volumes already fell three percent this year; one more blast and Marseille’s queues will triple.”citeturn0search0turn0search6 Hunt helped her cut the leads while Barakat covered the hatch.

Unexpected twist: a hostage. The tanker’s chief engineer, a Lebanese national, was strapped to a chair near the manifold, a satphone taped to his hand. “They wanted me to force the company to pay in crypto,” he stammered. Hunt cut him free. “You’re safe,” he said. “Stay behind the dogs.”

Leyla radioed the bridge. “Skiffs neutralized. Mines disarmed. Bring in the tow.” Commodore Drakos’ patrol boat slid alongside, a Greek flag snapping in the wind. “About time,” she quipped. “I was about to invoice France for overtime.”

MI5’s Saira Malik, patched in from London, confirmed the pirates’ wallets were linked to Blue Trireme’s last slush fund. “We traced the crypto to a bunker trading desk in Zhoushan,” she said. “Chimbusco is already under scrutiny. This gives Beijing a reason to cooperate.”citeturn0search5

On deck, Hunt addressed the surviving crew. “You’re clear,” he told them. “Athena and Thanos will sweep once more. After that, France escorts you to Fos-sur-Mer for inspection. Consider it a free security audit.”

The chief engineer, still shaking, asked, “Why help us?”

“Because Cedar Crown dies only when every ghost fleet dies,” Leyla answered. “Now go prep your engines.”

French commandos loaded the pirate bodies into body bags. No survivors. Commodore Drakos saluted Hunt across the railing. “Oil flows,” she said. “You kept your promise.”

As the Atlantique circled overhead, Maya came through the headset. “World’s watching,” she said. “France already leaked photos of the boarding to counter Russian propaganda. Also, Centrus is screaming about uranium shortages back in the States. Apparently saving fusion won’t solve their enrichment bottleneck.”citeturn1news12

Hunt looked west, where the setting sun turned the Med to molten gold. “One crisis at a time,” he said. “Oil today, uranium tomorrow.” He patted Athena’s head. “Good girl. Let’s go home.”

### 45. Hormuz Relay
Home had to wait. Within a week, the coalition redeployed east as the Strait of Hormuz tightened like a noose. Houthis had already lobbed missiles toward the *Sounion* in the Red Sea, and now an Iran-backed smuggling ring tried to slip Marine Atlas components through the main Gulf shipping channel by hiding them inside desalination plant modules. The U.S. Fifth Fleet asked for Cedar Coalition assistance—specifically, Leyla’s knack for spotting fake humanitarian cargo.

Hunt, Leyla, Barakat, Yael, and Noura flew to Bahrain, boarding a U.S. Navy expeditionary base ship where a full DEVGRU troop prepped Mark V boats and combatant craft CCAs. Athena and Thanos got their own air-conditioned kennel near the mission brief. Rear Admiral Reyes laid it out: “Blue Trireme tried to rebrand in Dubai. Their freighter *Shahrazad II* departs in six hours with counterfeit desal modules. If it hits open water, the parts vanish into Iran’s Bandar Abbas network. We intercept in the Strait of Hormuz, between Qeshm and the Omani coast. Rules of engagement: stop, search, and if resistance appears, sink them. SOCOM handles kinetic. Cedar Coalition handles identification and evidence.”

Leyla pulled up satellite images. “The fake modules use a different aluminum alloy,” she said. “Athena smells the coolant additives. Thanos tracks explosives. We board from a Mark V, backed by a SEAL platoon. Hunt and I go with them. Noura stays on the expeditionary base to monitor radiation trace.”

Night fell as two Mark V boats roared toward the Strait, rooster tails glowing under IR lights. Hunt and Leyla crouched between SEALs, MPXs ready. Bishop nudged Hunt. “You jump, you clear left. We stack on the desal crate.” Hunt nodded.

As they approached *Shahrazad II*, bright lanterns blinked on deck. The freighter’s captain blared over VHF, “This is humanitarian aid! Stay back!” Leyla rolled her eyes. “Same script,” she muttered. The Mark Vs split: one swung portside, the other starboard, while a CCA hovered in reserve with a Marine sniper team already dialing their EXACTO barrels to the ship’s running lights so they could bend .50-cal paths around antenna clusters if the smugglers tried to use human shields.citeturn2search0turn2search3

Athena and Thanos jumped first, paws thumping on steel. Athena sniffed a crate and barked. “Coolant additive confirmed,” Leyla relayed. Hunt tossed a flashbang into the cargo hold and followed the SEALs up the ladder. Gunfire sparked. “Contact, bridge!” Bishop shouted. Hunt returned fire, dropping a smuggler aiming an AR at Athena.

On the bridge, Leyla found the captain frantically trying to delete logs. She pistol-whipped him. “Humanitarian my ass,” she said. “Where’s the kill switch?”

“If we stop, missiles launch,” the captain gasped. Leyla didn’t flinch. “Then die tired,” she replied. Bishop double-tapped him before he could reach a concealed detonator. No mercy, per policy.

Below decks, Hunt and Yael cracked open a “desal” crate to reveal Marine Atlas heat exchangers lacquered black. Noura confirmed via satlink: “Traces match Calliope’s files. You found their Gulf cache.”

Unexpected complication: Iranian Revolutionary Guard patrol boats sped toward the scene, lights dark. Admiral Reyes radioed, “Hold fire unless they do. Omani liaison negotiating.” For tense minutes, the Mark Vs formed a cordon while Oman’s Coast Guard hailed the Iranians. Finally, the patrol boats peeled away—Tehran decided a firefight over smugglers wasn’t worth it.

With the heat exchangers confiscated and the freighter impounded, the coalition escorted *Shahrazad II* to Duqm, Oman, where EU inspectors awaited. U.S. Navy snipers provided overwatch from a destroyer’s deck, testing the same EXACTO guidance stack against swells and recording telemetry for DARPA’s engineers between shots.citeturn2search3

Back aboard the expeditionary base, Leyla scratched Athena’s ears. “You’ve sniffed more reactors than most IAEA inspectors,” she joked.

Hunt exhaled, finally allowing himself to think about Mykonos. “We killed the shipwrights, the bankers, the pilots, the engineers, the pirates, and now the Gulf smugglers,” he said. “Cedar Crown’s map is blank.”

“Until someone draws a new one,” Noura cautioned.

“Then we redraw faster,” Hunt replied. He lifted Saint Barbara’s icon toward the Gulf horizon. “But for tonight, we rest.” The Mark V bobbed beside the giant base ship, Athena curled up between the SEALs, and the Strait of Hormuz flowed quiet, at least for one night.

### 46. Mykonos Debrief
Days later, the coalition finally made it to Mykonos. Leyla insisted on a proper Greek thank-you: a long table at a taverna in Ano Mera, whitewashed walls, blue shutters, and a view of the Aegean that looked almost innocent. Katerina from the Hellenic Arsenal shipped in a platter of grilled octopus and stuffed vine leaves, while Eleni Stavros (the actual officer, not the tanker) presented a magnum of Assyrtiko from her family vineyard.

Hunt, Leyla, Noura, Barakat, Yael, Athena, Thanos, Tarek (who flew in from Jordan), Eleni, Commodore Drakos, Saira Malik, Maya’s holo projector, and even Bishop and Vandal from DEVGRU crowded around the table. Plates of grilled lamb, saganaki, and horiatiki salad disappeared fast. Thanos begged for souvlaki scraps until Leyla relented.

Eleni raised her glass. “To saints, sailors, and stubborn engineers,” she toasted. “And to the fact that Mykonos still has bars.”

“Only because we bought this one for the night,” Leyla quipped. “Hunt’s paying.”

Hunt lifted his own glass of chilled rosé. “To Cedar Coalition,” he said. “And to the cities that let us fight for them.”

Noura clinked glasses with him. “And to the students who’ll never know how close they came to studying radioactive fallout,” she added.

Barakat tapped his glass loudly. “I have something,” he announced. “We’re officially naming the dogs honorary members of the Hellenic Coast Guard.” He produced tiny dog-sized life jackets embroidered with Greek flags. Athena barked; Thanos knocked over a wine glass with his tail.

Maya’s holo flickered. “While you all eat, the portal just hit two million subscribers,” she said. “Half of them came during Black Tide. Transparency wins.”

Tarek leaned back, chewing on feta. “You promise me one thing, ya Sam,” he said to Hunt. “Next time we smuggle anything, it better be dessert.”

Hunt smiled, genuinely. “Deal,” he said.

As the night wore on, Eleni taught Barakat to say “cheers” in Greek, Yael challenged Bishop to a backgammon match, Maya streamed live footage of Athena sleeping under the table, and Leyla danced barefoot on the flagstones. The war wasn’t over—wars never really ended—but for one night, Greek food and wine wrapped the coalition in warmth.

Saint Barbara’s icon sat in the center of the table, propped against a bottle of ouzo. When the bells of the nearby church chimed midnight, Hunt touched the icon and whispered, “We’ll be ready.”

### 47. Stillness in Syros
For once, they actually took a few days off as promised. The coalition rented a cluster of stone cottages on Syros, quieter than Mykonos but close enough for support if the world exploded again. Leyla spent day one asleep on a terrace lounger, a straw hat over her face, Athena curled against her legs. Noura joined local fishermen at dawn, relearning the patience of casting nets. Barakat explored the island’s tunnels with Eleni, cataloging WWII graffiti for the arsenal archives. Yael disappeared into an old record shop and emerged with vinyls she insisted would soothe even Spartan commandos.

Hunt tried to rest. He hiked the hill above Ermoupoli, overlooking the harbor. For the first hour he counted sails instead of threats. For the second hour he answered old voicemails from Maya, Tarek, and his Jerusalem contacts, each message ending with “rest” or “drink water.” On day three, he finally did nothing—sat on a rock, ate pistachios, watched a ferry depart, and let the Aegean wind erase the adrenaline.

On their final night, they gathered on the beach, lighting a bonfire with driftwood. Leyla played Greek folk songs on a borrowed bouzouki, badly but enthusiastically. Tarek roasted lamb over the flames, declaring it inferior to Jordanian mansaf but acceptable. Katerina brought bottles of island wine and jars of pistachio spoon sweet.

“This will last until the next crisis,” Leyla said, raising her cup.

“Then we load pistachios instead of reactors,” Hunt replied.

Athena and Thanos sprinted along the shoreline, chasing gulls. The sea glowed with bioluminescence under their paws, trailing blue fire. Noura leaned against the bonfire and whispered, “If we ever build a fusion lab in Greece, it’ll look like this.”

“Just add better music,” Yael teased.

They stayed until the embers died. When dawn came, Hunt packed Saint Barbara’s icon, rolled up his kit, and felt—for the first time in months—like he’d actually rested. The war would return. Cedar Crown’s remnants would mutate. But the few quiet days on Syros proved they could pause without forgetting why they fought.
### 48. Aden Pulse
The stillness of Syros shattered when Commodore Drakos patched through on the coalition mesh. Satellite overlays of the Internationally Recommended Transit Corridor glowed across the patio table, tanker icons blinking red where AIS tracks vanished near the Gulf of Aden. EU Aspides and Combined Maritime Forces intel converged on the same name—Black Fajr, a splinter outfit that lost its financing when Hunt torched Maverick’s Mediterranean accounts. Now they were hijacking bunkered crude to restock, timing raids to squeeze shippers already rattled by Houthi launches. Oil futures lurched three dollars in an hour; Beirut’s traders whispered about another insurance spiral. “They’re raiding the same seam we opened with Black Tide,” Leyla muttered, thumbing Saint Barbara’s icon. “We either shut them down or watch freight costs strangle Athens.”

Tarek joined via scrambler from Amman, relief in his voice. “Those extremists have chased our convoys since Wadi Sirhan. They want payback for the diamonds you seized in Morocco. They also grabbed a crate of Ottoman lockwork out of Lamu—your restorer friends are furious.” The detail stung. Hunt’s antique-weapons legend had been built on trust, and now Cedar Crown’s orphans were attacking the same curators who kept his cover alive. The Jerusalem Circuit pinged: Lamu Fort’s curator begged him to inspect the damaged matchlocks personally. It was the perfect cover for moving a kill team close to Somalia.

The op order unfolded fast. Phase I: fly to Kenya under UNESCO paperwork, audit the looted weapons, and let Kenyan Recce Company shepherd them toward the border. Phase II: stage an amphibious plane near the Kiunga Marine Reserve to shadow the pirate flotilla while DEVGRU and Danab battalion elements rode Mark V boats up the channel. Phase III: punch inland to Ras Kamboni caves, where Black Fajr stored cash, weapons, and hostages. “FIDO all the way,” Barakat said. “We drive on whether or not the corridor cooperates.” Hunt nodded, scribbling new call signs—Seawolf flight would return with a four-person MH-60S det, while an MH-60R from HSM-74’s detachment would manage Link 16 feeds over the shipping channel.

*Illustration: Horn of Africa ISR routes (IRTC → Kiunga → Ras Kamboni).* 

### 49. Lamu Relay
They landed in Lamu under the guise of inspecting the fort’s museum. Salt air mixed with frankincense as Hunt traced the gouge across an 18th-century Ottoman snaplock musket. “Black Fajr’s boys pried out the gold inlay,” the curator whispered. Hunt promised a restoration once the parts were recovered, his mind cataloging every scratch the way Gabriel Alon once cataloged canvases. Leyla swapped forged customs forms for access to the old dhows, loading crates of “replacement springs” that actually hid sensor buoys and sugar-gel cartridges. Athena and Thanos lounged like pets in the courtyard, but their ears tracked every footfall.

Black Fajr struck before dusk. A Hilux full of gunmen roared down the causeway, spraying the museum walls. Hunt and Leyla vaulted into their armored Prado, engines roaring as they bolted through Lamu’s alleys. “SNAFU already,” Leyla hissed, as a skiff of reinforcements cut them off near Manda Island. Hunt jinked through mangroves, but the enemy truck clipped the Prado’s tail, sending them fishtailing toward the water. “FISH,” Barakat said over comms. “Forget the dents—shit happens.” They gunned it anyway. Kenyan Recce Company allies erupted from a camouflaged checkpoint, PKMs sparking. In seconds, the ambushers were pinned, and a Recce MATV smashed the Hilux, ending the chase in a fountain of sand. The surviving gunmen tried to bolt into town, but Athena and Thanos dragged two down while Kenyan officers cuffed the rest. Recce’s Major Ayele tossed Hunt a data puck recovered from the truck—ledger fragments showing crypto wallets linked directly to Maverick’s frozen Maltese accounts. “All yours,” she said. “Now go find the crates they stole.”

With the city secure, Hunt met a clandestine buyer in the Shela dunes—a Syndicate broker who’d agreed to fence rare rifle components back to Leyla. In exchange for shutting down the pirates, he handed over a mahogany chest full of Chassepot firing pins and Indo-Greek damascene plates the Hellenic arsenal had requested months ago. The side trip kept their antique-restorer cover intact, and Leyla smirked. “We leave with more than we brought,” she said. “Even our legends get paid.”

### 50. Dhow Gauntlet
Phase II launched before dawn. A Twin Otter on amphibious floats skimmed the Kiunga shallows, engines coughing in the mist. Hunt, Leyla, Barakat, and Yael rode strapped to cargo netting beside crates of relief supplies they would later donate. The Otter splashed down ten nautical miles off Pate Island, masquerading as a survey plane while streaming video of the pirate convoy creeping north. “Black Fajr mothership plus four skiffs,” Yael reported. “Thermal pings mark satchel charges on the tanker’s rails.”

Mark V and Combatant Craft Assault boats roared beneath them, SEAL platoon MACO-3 stacked in full kits—no two-man fantasy pairs here, just fire teams backed by Danab commandos. Overhead, Seawolf flight returned with two MH-60S Armed Helo Seahawks loaded with GAU-21s and four AGM-114 Hellfires, while an MH-60R orbiting higher fed Link 16 tracks to the Otter and a French FREMM on the horizon. “Seawolf-One, crew of four, entering gun-run,” the HAC called. Door gunners hosed the lead skiff, .50-cal streams chiseling wood. When the mothership tried to turn toward the shipping channel, Seawolf-Two’s weapons officer announced, “Hellfire ripple, targets Fajr-One, Fajr-Two,” and both missiles slammed down, turning the dhow’s engine room into spray.

Hunt and Leyla popped the Otter’s side hatch, launching spear drones that wrapped the tanker’s rudder cables so the pirates couldn’t steer into oncoming traffic. The Otter’s pilot then gunned the floats, skipping across the swell until they could drop Barakat and Yael by RHIB onto the tanker’s stern. “FUBAR scenario averted,” Barakat grinned as the SEALs breached. “Black Tide taught them not to test us.”

### 51. Ras Kamboni Siege
Intelligence from captured radios pointed inland to Ras Kamboni—a honeycomb of WWII-era Italian bunkers Black Fajr had converted into vaults and cages. Hunt’s convoy rolled through thorn scrub under cover of a dust storm. Danab operators led the assault, with DEVGRU’s Blue Squadron stacking behind them and Kenyan Recce mortars zeroed on the cave mouths. Athena and Thanos wore Kevlar vests and nose cams, eager for the chase.

Leyla’s EXACTO-equipped Barretts anchored overwatch while MI5 liaison Saira Malik served as JTAC, clearing Seawolf flight for another gun run. Two MH-60S helos dropped fast ropes, each crew of four rotating between GAU-21 suppression and hoist duties, while the MH-60R held high, calling out movers trying to break toward the beach. “Targets exfil south,” the Romeo’s TACCO warned. “Rogue Toyota plus mortar trailer.” Hunt ordered a FIDO push—“drive on, pinch them in the wash.”

Inside the caverns, the fight turned knife-close. Pirate financiers barricaded themselves behind crates of diamonds and stolen weapons, screaming that Cedar Crown had ruined them. Barakat lobbed a thermobaric charge, concussing the tunnel so Danab could breach. Athena and Thanos flushed hostages toward the exit, teeth bared at anyone reaching for a detonator. Outside, SEALs guided EXACTO rounds down ventilation shafts, the seeker dots bouncing green as the bullets curved along S-turns to drop gunners crouched behind blast doors. When a battered technical tried to bolt for the dunes, Seawolf-One’s crew chief stitched it with GAU-21 fire while Seawolf-Two’s pilot fired a single Hellfire into a cave portal, collapsing the tunnel mouth without touching the hostages. Minutes later, ALL Fajr shooters were down. “No negotiations,” Hunt reminded everyone. “We were invited to kill terrorists, not parley.”

### 52. Jubba Dividend
When the dust settled, pallets of cash, uncut diamonds, and stolen antique parts filled the Ras Kamboni courtyard. Kenyan intelligence counted thirty-six million dollars’ worth of contraband, not including the rare matchlocks and damascene plates Leyla gathered for Athens. EU Aspides wired congratulations; Combined Maritime Forces announced that the main shipping channel was clear again. The coalition redirected a portion of the seized funds to rebuild the Lamu museum and finance the Cedar Portal’s whistleblower bounties. Another tranche went to the Hellenic Arsenal and Commodore Drakos, ensuring Hunt’s next restoration contract in Athens had legitimate funding.

Tarek arrived a day later with Turkish coffee and a grin. “You didn’t just wipe a pirate crew,” he said. “You erased the last extremist bankroll tied to Maverick’s Sahara phase. Greece saw the feed—you’re expected in Athens to restore a cabinet of rare war pistols before they go on display for the government.” Hunt glanced at the crates of salvaged parts. “Guess Samuel Hunt the antique restorer lives another day,” he said. Leyla raised an eyebrow. “And Samuel Hunt the hunter?”

“He’s wheels up for Greece once we refuel,” Hunt replied. “But first, we pay the Danab, the Recce, and every ally who dragged us out of that FUBAR car chase. Then we brief Maya—those Black Fajr ledgers tie straight into the Greek diamonds cell we saw hints of in Book Two.”

As dusk fell over the Jubba River delta, Athena and Thanos sprawled in the sand, bellies full. Seawolf flight banked overhead, heading back to their destroyer. Hunt traced the map from Kiunga to Ras Kamboni, noting every footprint, every allied position. They had wiped the pirates out in five relentless chapters, just as promised. Now the coalition pivoted toward Athens, where an invitation from the Greek government—and the specter of Book Two’s fusion-hostage plot—waited like a fresh fuse.


### 53. Acropolis Intake
The Greek government rolled out a red carpet made of rusted weapons. Hunt, Leyla, Noura, and Barakat stepped into the Hellenic Ministry of Culture’s subterranean arsenal beneath Piraeus, where curators in lab coats hovered over antique pistols earmarked for the National War Museum. "You arrive as restorers, you leave as ghosts," Deputy Minister Eleni Stavros joked, ushering Hunt toward a tray of Ottoman flintlocks scarred by Black Fajr pry marks. Hunt slid on magnifying lenses, tracing each gouge. "They used screwdriver pries, not chisels. Same crew that hit Lamu," he confirmed, giving the Greek narrative cover they needed.

Greek intelligence officers from EYP unrolled satellite prints of the Saronic Gulf. A red ellipse circled a government weapons facility on Salamis Island—where cedar crates of newly restored pistols would be displayed for donors in three nights. "Maverick’s Greek node will strike there to steal titanium housings for their next fusion attempt," Eleni said. "We want you in the gallery as honored restorers while our RAID equivalent—ETA—locks the perimeter." Hunt nodded, thinking like Gabriel Alon’s darker twin: antique restorer outside, DEVGRU liaison inside.

Leyla unpacked the recovered Chassepot firing pins, fitting them into velvet-lined cases with a restorer’s care. "Our legend stays pristine," she murmured. "No one suspects a smuggler who files provenance reports." Barakat briefed a Hellenic Navy SOF detachment on Cedar slang—FUBAR, FIDO, EXACTO—so the joint force would share shorthand once bullets flew. Noura calibrated radiation sniffers disguised as humidity gauges, while Athena and Thanos curled beside the pistols like loyal guardians.

*Illustration: Piraeus weapons gallery layout and Salamis approach lanes.*

### 54. Arsenal Echo
Three nights later, the restored pieces glittered under glass as diplomats sipped Assyrtiko. Hunt wore linen and a lapel pin shaped like Saint Barbara; Leyla shimmered in a curator’s gown hiding ceramic plates.
